## Page 39

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 4: Research Methods by Laura Short, Ph.D. Research Design for Artifact Analysis Archeological data recovery excavations at Waxahachie Creek-1 site 41EL246 were carried out according to field protocols approved by the Texas Historical Commission (THC) and TxDOT Environmental Affairs Division (ENV) under Texas Antiquities Permit Number 8694. Following the fieldwork conducted in June and July 2019, an analytical research design was prepared by Versar and was approved by TxDOT ENV. This research design was used to guide both the laboratory analysis and reporting within this document. The text that follows was derived from the research design and provides a summary of the basic laboratory techniques that were applied to the artifact collection. Additional specialized laboratory methods are provided in subsequent chapters for analyses that are outside of the scope of the basic treatment of all artifacts in the collection. Two historic contexts from Archeology in the Eastern Planning Region, Texas: A Planning Document (Kenmotsu and Perttula 1993) are applicable for framing the analysis of the materials from 41EL246. These contexts are: Quaternary Environments and Archeology in Northeast Texas and Hunter-Gatherer Mobility. Concepts outlined in the planning document were synthesized into four research domains for this study: Research Domain 1: Site Chronology and Site Integrity Research Domain 2: Settlement Patterns Research Domain 3: Paleoenvironment Research Domain 4: Subsistence Systems Research Domain 5: Material Culture and Technological Change Each research domain, associated issues, and specific investigative strategies are presented below. Research Domain 1: Site Chronology and Site Integrity Site 41EL246 lacks diagnostic artifacts and the recent excavations indicate that the initial estimate of Late Prehistoric (A.D. 700 to 1700) age for the site may be incorrect. Therefore, it is vital to the interpretation of the site that a primary effort focuses on refining the site’s chronology and establishing the integrity of the studied proveniences. Below we present three research questions that address the chronology and integrity of the site. Because site chronology and contextual integrity is a central issue to the validity of the interpretations of the site, we feel that it is best to try to answer these questions in tandem using multiple lines of evidence and investigative strategies. These relate to research topics outlined in the scope of work, including the contextual integrity and arrangement of the sediments; the timing of deposition, development, and burial of the West Fork soil; and cultural and absolute chronology indicators as they relate to the site’s chronology, and specifically the cotemporality, or not, of the features, through vertical isolation of discrete habitation zone(s), 23


---

## Page 40

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 noting human activity areas as related horizontal inter-site spatial patterning of macro- and microartifacts. Research Question: What is the chronology of the site? No time-diagnostic artifacts were recovered from previous subsurface investigations at 41EL246. A tentative date of Late Prehistoric (A.D. 700 to 1700) was estimated based on analogous archeological sites in the region. These had similar materials near the top of the West Fork Soils, including cultural materials dating to the Late Prehistoric (Ferring 1986, 1990; Lebo and Brown 1990; Brown and Lebo 1991; Pemberton and Cliff 2008:33). 41EL86, an isolated find of a prehistoric chert cobble core exists approximately 6 mi (9.8 kilometers [km]) southwest of 41EL246 (Chada and Weston 1990), and 41EL151, a possible late prehistoric bison kill exists approximately 8 mi (12.9 km) north of 41EL246 (Jurney et al. 1992). The more recent geoarcheological investigation of the site has indicated, however, that the soils are unlikely to be West Fork, and therefore that analogy is not valid. Therefore, multiple radiocarbon dates will help establish a chronological context for the site. Items suitable for radiocarbon dating will be extracted from the macrobotanical assemblage (i.e. from flotation samples), faunal collection, and the microartifact column. In total 10 samples will be submitted for dating. This will include 5 samples from the microartifact column, 1 sample of charcoal recovered from Feature 1 flotation samples, and 4 bone samples. The 4 bone samples will include one burnt bone which showed possible human activity, one bone sample associated with the lithic flakes, and a bone sample from the high point and low point of the site stratigraphy. Research Question: What is the depositional history of the site? Initial field observations during the geoarcheological trench and block excavation at site 41EL246 did not display distinct stratigraphy. After the the eastern wall was uncovered, however, a surprsing complex deposition sequence was readily apparent in the profile. Careful geoarcheological examination indicated that the deposits comprise a body of subtly different color and texture sediments on the dark grayish brown color continuum. Thirty-two strata were identified, though this likely underestimates the number of physically distinct deposits present. These deposits dip and thin to the south, away from the Waxahachie Creek channel, revealing a deposit that resembles a levee immediately adjacent to the creek (northern side) flanked by a swale (southern side). Geomorophological analysis including physical charaterization of the deposits and analysis of the SfM photography will be used to determine the depositional history of the site. Research Question: What is the stratigraphic integrity of the cultural deposits at the site? Site 41EL246 exists along a creek bank with evidence of water flow, possible surface burns, and a several modern trash pit that may have also been burned. While the cultural material suggests there are at least three analytical units, including the modern trash pits, the cooking feature, a cluster of faunal bones and a light lithic scatter, the integrity of these deposits is 24


---

## Page 41

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 not clear. The faunal material and cooking feature have clearly been affected by water flowing over the surface, the extent of this disturbance is unknown. Geoarcheological analysis, including microartifact analysis and paleomagnetic analysis near the cooking feature to assess thermal alteration, will help determine how intact the site is. Because the stratigraphy is relatively complex, and the stratigraphy is dipping (i.e., not consistent by elevation across the site), it will be difficult to correlate cultural deposits across the site to the stratigraphy seen in the eastern profile. Research Domain 2: Settlement Patterns Cultural remains at 41EL246 are sparse; however, ecofacts, which can speak to the paleoenvironment are plentiful. In the scope of work, one of the research topics was settlement patterns regarding site types, distributions, exchange/interaction, and seasonality on the landscape as related to the seasonal and paleoenvironmental reconstruction of the depositional environment of the West Fork soil. Seasonality cannot be addressed with the material remains collected from the site and is therefore not addressed here. The sparse cultural material present at 41EL246 is unlikely to provide much information regarding site types and distribution or exchange. Research Question: How does the site tie into settlement patterns of the region? The small cooking feature and minimal lithic materials indicates that the site was likely occupied for a relatively short time period. This could be indicative of a mobile settlement strategy or it could be a temporary nonresidential site, such a campsite for resource gathering or hunting. Dating the site, via radiocarbon analysis, should help provide context for interpretation of a temporary site in relation to what people were doing in the region. Research Domain 3: Paleoenvironment As stated in Research Domain 2, cultural remains at 41EL246 are sparse; however, ecofacts, which can speak to the paleoenvironment are plentiful. Research Question: What is the paleoenvironment of the site? The site does not appear to provide much information available regarding human behavior; however, it does provide a wealth of paleoenvironmental information. The paleoenvironment can be reconstructed through faunal analysis, including both bone and snails, as well as macrobotanical analysis, and informed by the geomorphological analysis. For both faunal and vegetal analysis, each specimen will be identified to species level, if possible, or to the most descriptive taxonomic level possible. Some species have narrow ecological requirements and are linked to specific environmental conditions; snails in particular are good indicators of environment. The faunal assemblage consisting of bone recovered during excavation will be submitted to Mr. Jeremy Loven for analysis. The snail assemblage recovered from the Feature 1 flotation samples and microartifact column will also be submitted to Dr. Manuel R. Palacios-Fest for 25


---

## Page 42

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 analysis. Due to time constraints and the large number of snail recovered from the microartifact column analysis of the entire collection is likely not possible. Research Domain 4: Subsistence Systems The cultural material present at 41EL246 predominantly relates to subsistence activities. It should, therefore, provide evidence regarding subsistence and culinary patterns indicated by lithic analysis, faunal taxa, macrobotanical remains, and cultural alterations of faunal material, as evidence of specific cultural uses, including procurement, processing, and consumption by the inhabitants of 41EL246. While there is limited lithic material, botanical and faunal analysis will help elucidate questions regarding subsistence systems. Research Question: How does this site tie into subsistence patterns of the region? Faunal and botanical material from the site should be informative regarding subsistence activities. Understanding the site function is tied directly to understanding subsistence patterns. Faunal and macrobotanical analysis will contribute to that. This will focus on the bones and not snails, as most snails were unlikely to have been consumed. In addition to identifying taxonomic level, the bones will be analyzed for taphonomic characteristics, such as burning or cut marks, which will indicate if they were present on the site due to human activity such as hunting or processing. Botanical material recovered from the cooking feature may indicate if the oven was used to cook plant material and potentially reveal the types of wood used for fuel. Five flotation light fractions from Feature 1 will be submitted to Dr. Leslie Bush for analysis. Incorporating information regarding time period and environment will help give context to the site interpretation, which in turn can be tied into regional patterns. Research Domain 5: Material Cultural and Technological Change A relatively small artifact assemblage was recovered from site 41EL246; however, it can still speak to Socio-Technological patterns regarding archeological features, artifact forms, manufacturing processes, and production methods, with subsistence implications regarding reconstructed tool uses and local raw materials. Research Question: What are the characteristics of the chipped lithic assemblage recovered from the site? Though sparse, a characterization of the lithic assemblage is necessary for a discussion of the site as a whole. Our analysis of the lithic assemblage will follow the protocols laid out in the TxDOT Chipped Stone Analytical Protocol and the TxDOT Protocol for Debitage Analysis. The entire assemblage of chipped stone tools will be analyzed according to the dendritic taxonomy developed by TxDOT to record artifact modes deemed vital for contributing to the state-wide database, allowing for intrasite comparisons. We will additionally record the relevant metric and nonmetric attributes of chipped stone tools as a component of the linear lithic reduction model developed by TxDOT. For the debitage analysis, we will employ the TxDOT mass analysis strategy. Additionally, analysis of the lithic material source may touch on mobility, exchange, or both. 26


---

## Page 43

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Research Question: What are the characteristics of the fire cracked rock recovered from the site? Only one prehistoric cooking feature (preliminary analytical unit) was encountered on the site; however, a large number of FCR were distributed across the site. The analysis of FCR, sorting by material type and size, can give information about the cycle of rock use and reuse in ovens. Notably, the stones in the oven were relatively small but not all presented indications of fragmentation, while FCR in the rest of the site was significantly larger. 27

## Page 45

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 5: Field Methods by Laura Short, Ph.D. Field Investigations Data recovery excavations at the Waxahachie Creek-1 site were conducted in three major stages using a feature-focused approach. Prior to the initiation of data recovery, a skid steer mulcher was used to clear the overgrowth and an approximately 40-in-diameter pecan tree in preparation for archeological investigations (Figure 3). The overgrowth and pecan tree were mulched onsite with the stump left intact. This cleared area served as the project limit for all subsequent investigations. Global positioning system (GPS) mapping of 41EL246 within the right-of-way was used to construct an overall site map, with plan and profile general views, produced with GPS data rectified to aerial photographs and topographic maps. The second stage consisted of the excavation of a 20-m-long by 6-m-wide geoarcheological trench (Trench 6) using a Gradall machine (Figures 4 and 5). As potential features were encountered, they were bisected and hand-excavated. For the third stage, following the excavation of Trench 6, a 20-m-long by 10-m-wide block was excavated using a Gradall (Figure 6). Features encountered in this block were hand excavated in 1-by-1-m test units, in 10-centimeter (cm) arbitrary levels. Though the work authorization stipulates that 10 cubic meters (m3) was to be hand excavated, no features were encountered. Due to the lack of observed stratigraphy in Trench 6, the 50-by-50-cm microartifact sample column and associated magnetic susceptibility samples were not placed in the trench, but in the eastern wall of the block where stratigraphy was observed (Figure 7). After the eastern wall of the block was cleared, SfM photogrammetry was done to document the stratigraphy along that profile. 29

Geoarcheological Trench Excavations Initially, the geoarcheological trench (Trench 6) was stipulated to be 2 m long and 2 m wide; however, it was expanded to 6 m wide to meet safety regulations requiring 2-m benches. The northern end was extended somewhat as well, in order to fully excavate Feature 1. The final trench was approximately 21 by 6 m, not including a ramp for entering and exiting, as well as some safety sloping along the upper walls on the northern and eastern side (see Figure 5). This trench was oriented north-south, approximately perpendicular to Waxahachie Creek. It extended through some portion of Trench 4 from the previous 2008 investigation (see Figure 2); however, the boundaries of that trench could not be defined in the sediments during the current investigation. Trench 6 was excavated with a Gradall in approximately 10-cm levels to a depth of approximately 3.7 m below surface. As potential features were encountered, they were bisected and hand-excavated with shovels and trowels. Along the northern wall, a small scatter of burned rocks was encountered (Feature 1). Several other surface stains were excavated as features; however, upon bisection, it was determined that they were root burns. Any potential features, artifacts, and ecofacts were mapped with a total station as they were encountered. Block Excavation Following the excavation of Trench 6, a 20-by-10-m block was excavated using a Gradall in 10-cm levels. This block was oriented parallel and somewhat overlapping Trench 6, removing the bench from Trench 6 for safety purposes (see Figure 6). Features encountered in this 32

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 block were hand excavated in 1-by-1-m test units, in 10-cm arbitrary levels. A total station was used to establish both vertical and horizontal datums and to record large artifacts. The total station was set up over the same ground surface datum each day and two back sight measurements were collected to account for small errors in data collection. With the exception of bulk soil collected from feature fill or for specialized analyses, all hand- excavated matrix was screened onsite through 6.4-millimeter (mm; 0.25-in) mesh hardware cloth. Lithic tools and debitage, FCR, baked clay masses, animal bone, and mussel shell fragments were collected from the screens. Due to its extremely fragmented nature, charcoal was not systematically collected from the screens. Field observations were documented on standard Versar forms for each test unit level and separate forms were used for each feature encountered. Flotation samples were collected from all potential features encountered, and additional bulk samples were collected from profile walls and feature contexts. All recovered materials were bagged, labeled, and delivered to the Versar laboratory (see Laboratory Procedures below). 33

## Page 50

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Geoarcheological Investigations The geoarcheological investigations at the site began with the excavation of Trench 6 on the western side of the proposed block. Unfortunately, making this trench safe to enter had been overlooked and doing so increased the excavation time and width of the trench significantly. Although a small part of this trench was drawn, the task of assessing the site stratigraphy was shifted to the eastern side of the block near the end of the excavation. This shift was due to the lack of stratigraphy apparent in the eastern wall of Trench 6. As a result, plans to record the eastern wall of Trench 6 using SfM also shifted to focusing on the eastern wall of the block. The original plan to record the stratigraphy of the site with a SfM image was eventually fulfilled and proved to be of great assistance, as the block exhibited more prominent stratigraphy than anticipated. Indeed, the combination of dipping strata and recording the deposits from two vertical faces offset by a 4-ft horizontal bench is just the scenario envisioned when the SfM imaging of the profile was proposed. The eastern profile of the block was drawn by hand, but a line drawing of this profile derived from annotation of the SfM image resulted in a more accurate rendering of the surprisingly complex depositional sequence. The deposits exposed by the block excavation comprise a body of subtly different color and texture sediments on the dark grayish brown color continuum, and 32 strata were identified along the eastern wall (and this accounting undoubtedly underestimates the actual number of physically distinct deposits present). The sandier sediments are slightly lighter colored whereas the finer textured ones are darker colored, and entire suite of deposits dip and thin to the south, away from the Waxahachie Creek channel, revealing a deposit that resembles a levee immediately adjacent to the creek (northern side) flanked by a swale (southern side) (see Figure 7). Most of the interfaces were scored on the wall with a trowel and can be seen clearly in this image if one examines it closely. Historic age refuse was observed within the swale sediments to a depth of more than a meter on the southern side of the block indicating the relative youth of almost half of the deposits exposed in this portion of the site. A column of samples was collected for microartifact analysis (n=54) and bulk characterization (n=89) from near the midpoint of the eastern block wall, and a sample was also collected from each deposit described in the stratigraphic succession, given that the column sample fails to encompass the entire suite of deposits. Although some of the darker colored deposits within this sequence may be pedogenically altered, the majority of the color is most likely detrital and attributable to darker colored clays. The initial assessment of the site as being Pilot Point alloformation and the “soil” at the top as the West Fork soil appears to be incorrect, as at least half of the deposits exposed by the block are demonstrably “recent” alluvium. In addition to the aforementioned samples, a small suite (n=21) of sediment collected in paleomagnetic cubes were collected through and adjacent to Feature 1 near the northern end wall of the excavation in order to assess the extent to which these deposits have been altered by exposure to heat. 34


---

## Page 51

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Laboratory Procedures All cultural materials collected from the data recovery investigations were returned to the Versar laboratory facilities in Richardson, Texas, to be washed, weighed, counted, catalogued, and labeled in compliance with CAS-TSU standards prior to analyses. Context and attribute data for all materials were recorded in a Microsoft Access database on an IBM network system. The main categories of material recovered were animal bone, shell, flaked lithics, botanical samples (both flotation samples and opportunistically collected wood charcoal), and FCR. Lithic tools and debitage, faunal materials, and FCR were analyzed in the Versar laboratory; shell, geoarcheological soil samples, and a subset of botanical remains were sent to external analysts. The following discussion describes analytical strategies and summarizes the attributes recorded during the analysis of each class of material. Botanical Remains Each feature encountered in the manual excavations was bisected, and half of the feature was bulk collected and floated to recover charred and uncharred plant remains. A standard oil-drum flotation apparatus with upward water flow—sometimes called a Siraf system—was employed to separate material of interest from extraneous matrix. The light fraction—charcoal fragments and other buoyant objects scooped from the water surface—was dried and then passed through 6.35- and 0.5-mm screens. The heavy fraction—material that sank to the bottom of the apparatus—was dried and passed through 6.35- and 1.6-mm screens. Heavy- fraction objects larger than 6.35 mm were sorted into material classes (FCR, lithics, bone, etc.) and integrated into the corresponding collections from the excavations. Heavy materials smaller than 6.35 mm were set aside for later discard, with the exception of bone fragments and lithics, which were integrated into the appropriate assemblages with the larger pieces. The fine and coarse components of the light fraction material from each context were examined by Versar personnel using hand lenses and microscopes as necessary. Professional examination of the light fraction from Feature 1 was undertaken by Dr. Leslie Bush, and the identifications and their implications are discussed in Chapter 10. Stone Tools and Debitage Versar personnel analyzed the lithic artifacts, including the one biface and debitage. Because there were very few chipped stone artifacts, this analysis is necessarily limited in scope. The chipped stone artifact category includes all chipping or flaking debris and intentionally chipped or flaked stone artifacts. Prehistoric chipped stone artifacts, likewise, include several classes of tools representing different uses and functions. Debitage refers to the waste flakes and angular material (shatter) generated during the manufacture and maintenance of chipped stone tools. In some cases, these flakes served as expedient tools for a variety of cutting, scraping, or perforating functions. All lithic artifacts were classified to the most specific morphofunctional class possible, and counts, weights, and raw material types were recorded for each artifact or group of similar artifacts from each provenience. 35


---

## Page 52

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Fire-Cracked Rock FCR was subjected to a similar basic analysis in the Versar laboratory. Fragments were first sorted based by rock material type (e.g., sandstone, limestone, quartzite), and any direct evidence of heating (e.g., fracture pattern, discoloration) noted (see Chapter 9). Within material types, the FCR was then sorted into size classes: smaller than 0.5 in (12.7 mm), 0.5 to 1 in (12.7 to 25.4 mm), 1 to 1.5 in (25.4 to 38.1 mm), 1.5 to 2 in (38.1 to 50.8 mm), 2 to 4 in (50.8 to 101.6 mm), 4 to 6 in (101.6 to 152.4 mm), and larger than 6 in (152.4 mm). The cumulative weight and total number of FCR fragments within each class and within each archeological context were then recorded in the project Access database. Faunal Remains There was copious animal bone recovered from 41EL246; however, the majority of it was very fragile, and much of it could not be removed without significant fragmentation. Similar to other material classes, basic counts and weights were recorded for the analytical categories of these materials. The bone specimens were separated by the highest identifiable taxonomic level possible. However, in most cases the highly fragmentary nature of the collection afforded only very basic classification: small terrestrial mammal (STM), medium terrestrial mammal (MTM), large terrestrial mammal (LTM), and very large terrestrial mammal (VLTM). Assessments were also made of the following characteristics: bone element, burning/butchery evidence, and weathering evidence. Shell Shells including land snails and mussels were collected for analysis. The majority of the shell recovered came from the microartifact column. Shell was also recovered from the flotation samples from Feature 1. Due to time constraints only a portion of the shell was analyzed. Six samples from the microartifact column and the shell recovered from the Feature 1 flotation was analyzed. Due to the abundance of shell from the microartifact column a sample of 25 percent of each of the six samples was actually analyzed. While land snails were scattered throughout the site and deposits, only a subsample was collected for analysis. Mussel and other bivalve shells were also collected, though they were very fragile and did not always remain intact. Shell was identified to highest identifiable taxonomic level possible, and counts and weights were recoded for these categories. Curation Upon completion of the analyses and reporting, collected materials will be assessed for the appropriateness for long-term curation. Some classes of recovered remains, such as burned rocks, residual and bulk unprocessed soil samples, unidentifiable shell fragments, nonartifacts from the heavy and light fractions of sediment columns (e.g., coarse sand, gravel, rootlets), unburned (e.g., likely modern) macrobotanical remains, mass-produced historic period artifacts less than 50 years of age, etc., will be earmarked for discard. A list of such materials will be prepared and submitted to the THC for approval before disposal. Artifact inventories will be annotated to reflect the disposal of approved materials. 36


---

## Page 53

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 The remaining collections from the project will be prepared for curation according to the packaging guidelines established by CAS-TSU. Field and laboratory forms will be on acid-free paper; negatives and photographs will be labeled and placed in acid-free holders; cleaned and labeled artifacts will be placed in appropriate bags and boxes suitable for long-term storage. An inventory of materials submitted to curation will be prepared, and a copy will be sent with the collection to CAS-TSU for long-term storage. 37

## Page 55

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 6: Data Recovery Results by Laura Short, Ph.D. As a result of these investigations, 260 m3 of site matrix were excavated. This included 60 m3 from the geoarcheological trench (Trench 6) and 200 m3 from the archeological block. Nine features were tentatively identified; six were determined to be root burns, two were documenting the historic trash pit, and one was the result of prehistoric human activity. Features in the geoarcheological trench were bisected and hand-excavated but units were not placed; features located in the excavation block did have units placed for feature excavation. A total of five excavation units were placed, and approximately 1 m3 of sediment was hand- excavated from those units. A total of 1,454 prehistoric and 97 historic artifacts and 5,001 grams (g) of ecofacts (including charred vegetal remains, animal bone, and shell) were recovered from these excavations (Figure 9). The sections that follow detail the findings of the data recovery investigations and provide analyses of the features and radiocarbon dates obtained from these results. The laboratory dating results are presented in Appendix E and the full analytical tables for the artifact collection are provided in Appendix F. Excavations The excavations stage of data recovery took place between June 3 and July 3, 2019, and proceeded in two phases. First, a 20-by-6-m geoarcheological trench (Trench 6) was excavated using a Gradall machine. As potential features were encountered, they were bisected and hand-excavated, but 1-by-1-m test units were not placed. Following the excavation of Trench 6, a 20-by-10-m block was excavated using a Gradall. Features encountered in this block were to be hand-excavated in 1-by-1-m test units, in 10-cm arbitrary levels. Though the work authorization stipulates that 10 m3 were to be hand-excavated, no cultural features were encountered. Four surface stains were treated as potential features but only amounted to approximately 1 m3 total excavated by hand. A total of 8,377 artifacts were recovered from these excavations. Samples taken include sediment samples from feature for flotation, as well as magnetic susceptibility, and the microartifact column. Of the 28 feature sediment samples, five samples from Feature 1 were floated, contributing to the total artifact count. There were 1,447 FCR, 60 non-FCR stone, 1 chipped stone tool, 6 lithic debitage, 1,936 bone fragments (count is an approximation as the specimens continue to fragment), 4,830 shells and shell fragments (count is an approximation based on partial analysis), 35 charcoal samples, and 97 historic artifacts (see Appendix F). 39

## Page 57

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Feature and Unit Descriptions Feature 1 Feature 1 was initially encountered as a small FCR scatter in the northern wall (Figures 10 and 11). After fully excavated, the final dimensions of the feature was 80 by 100 cm, with the long axis oriented northwest, not including a “tail” of FCR extending another 40 cm east on the southern end. In the field, it was not clear whether this was actually a cultural feature or not – while FCR and some charcoal flecks were present, smooth pebbles were intermixed, and there was no clear stain that one would associate with a hearth pit or other cooking feature. Though no definitive evidence of burnt faunal or botanical remains were recovered, magnetic susceptibility support the interpretation of this as a small heating feature, possibly a single-use earth oven. Because this feature was partially embedded in the wall, it was not excavated in a similar manner as the other features. When first encountered, it was measured as approximately 20 by 40 cm in size, with the long axis oriented east-west. Part of the southern portion may have been removed by the Gradall, and the northern portion was still embedded in the wall. The portion of the feature that was accessible was fully exposed, and the southern edge was bisected to determine the depth of the feature. No staining or charcoal was encountered, and the feature was very thin – there appeared to be a single layer of rock. As Feature 1 appeared to extend into the northern wall, and it was becoming apparent this was the one feature most likely to be cultural in origin, it was decided that the northern side of the block needed to be expanded so that the feature could be fully exposed. Once it was fully exposed, the feature was bisected (Figures 12 through 14). Several soil samples were collected, as well as the FCR. Magnetic susceptibility samples were also collected across the feature. Artifacts recovered from Feature 1 include 3,043 shells, 1,216 FCR, 2 lithic flakes, 35 bone fragments, and 27 fragments of what appears to be iron. Other than a portion of the FCR, all the artifacts were recovered from flotation. Magnetic susceptibility samples were collected from the feature in order to determine if it is an in situ feature. If it is intact, there will be an increase in the magnetic susceptibility within the feature, and a decrease away from it. The results conform to this expectation. Therefore, the presence of the iron-like fragments are attributed to metal deposits that naturally occur in the Austin chalks or possibly contamination from the Gradall bucket. The majority of FCR from Feature 1 was limestone. In addition, there were some calcium carbonate nodules, and three pieces of calcite. Despite the large number of FCR, the volume of the feature was small, as 1,017 of the FCR were less than an inch in size. These are very small stones for an earth oven or stone boiling pit, considered to be at the end of their lifecycle. In addition to being small, many of the FCR from Feature 1 are round, as though they are water- worn, indicating they have not be reused multiple times (see Chapter 9). This indicates that it was heating feature that was used once, though it is not definitively used for cooking. The two flakes found in the flotation contribute evidence of human activity but are not informative to the function of this particular feature. They are two distal fragments of an unknown chert, one 0 to 6.7 mm (0 to 0.25 in) and the other 6.7 to 12.7 mm (0.25 to 0.5 in) in size. 41

## Page 60

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Figure 14. Profile of Feature 1 fully exposed after expanding Trench 6 to the north. Botanical and faunal material was recovered as well. Though wood specimens were recovered from the flotation samples, most of it was too small to be identified beyond the general category of hardwood. One piece is consistent with either mulberry or mesquite; the sample was too small to distinguish between the two. The identifiable faunal material consisted of shells and animal bones, also recovered from flotation. The shells included 14 land and 8 aquatic snails and 8 bivalves (five mussels, three clams). The bone fragments recovered from flotation were small unburnt fragments. Three are 6.7 to 12.7 mm (0.25 to 0.5 in) in size, one is 4.75 to 6.7 mm in size, nine are 2.36 to 4.75 mm in size, and 21 are smaller than 2.36 mm. Charcoal collected from a flotation sample, which specifically included the fragment of possible mulberry wood, from the feature was radiocarbon dated. This sample returned a conventional radiocarbon age of 2,440±30 BP (751 to 408 B.C.), dating Feature 1 to the Late Archaic. This date is significantly older than the lowest occupation identified in the microartifact column, which indicates a possible discontinuity in the stratigraphy or that a piece of old wood was dated. Feature 1 is a single-use heating feature dating to the Late Archaic. It represents an ephemeral occupation, likely by a small group of people. It is not clear what, if anything, was cooked in the heating feature. Feature 2 Feature 2 was encountered as an amorphous dark stain, approximately 110 by 80 cm in size in the geomorphological trench (Trench 6) (Figure 15). Because it was in the trench, the feature was not excavated in a unit, per the scope of work. The feature was bisected, and a portion of the central core was collected for flotation (Figure 16). The diffuse outer boundary was a 10YR 4/3 silty clay loam, while the central, clearly defined core of the feature was a 10YR 3/3 silty clay loam. 44

## Page 61

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 The profile showed that the heated portion of the feature was undulating, the main portion being a “V” shape, and “tail” extending from the top of the feature downwards to the west. It was initially thought this may be a small hearth, as there was burnt clay and an ashy matrix with charcoal flecks. According to the Geoarcheologist Charles Frederick, the presence of the burnt earth outside the main burned area is not consistent with a tree root; however, due to the tail and conical (rather than basin) shape of the profile, this was deemed to have been a root burn. In addition to the sediments for flotation, FCR and shells were collected from Feature 2. Three pieces of FCR, totaling 126.4 g in weight, were recovered from this feature: two FCR pieces are 1.5 to 2 in, one piece of FCR is 2 to 3 in; all are limestone. In addition, one Rabdotus mooreanus shell was recovered. This is a terrestrial snail species associated with semiarid grasslands. Figure 15. Planview of Feature 2. 45

Feature 3 Feature 3 was encountered as an amorphous stain, approximately 50 by 25 cm in size in the geomorphological trench (Trench 6) (Figure 17). Because it was in the trench, the feature was not excavated in a unit, per the scope of work. The feature was bisected, and a portion of the central core was collected for flotation (Figure 18). While the feature appears shallow in profile, as the surrounding matrix was excavated, a deeper element (Feature 3-B) of Feature 3 was exposed. This was a smaller circle of stained earth and burnt clay to the south of the excavation portion of Feature 3 (Figure 19). It appeared that the shape of the feature passed through matrix on a relatively steep angle, which was not visible when initially bisecting the feature (Figure 20). A sedimental sample was collected from Feature 3 for flotation. Though the exact Munsell color was not recorded in the field, the feature matrix is in the same dark brown to yellowish brown family that was seen in Feature 2. While there was burnt clay present, there were no cultural materials in the feature. Given the relatively thin, linear shape of the feature, and the lack of cultural materials, it was determined to most likely have been a root burn. 46

## Page 65

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Feature 4 Feature 4 was a large modern to historic trash pit, measuring 7.5 by 13.25 m, with the long axis oriented east-west (Figure 21). The debris in this pit appeared to have burned, as the sediment around it was discolored and there was melted glass present. The Munsell color for this feature was not recorded in the field, but the discolored sediments were in the black to dark brown range, and the lighter sediments in the center were more of a gray to grayish brown color. The full extent of the pit was not known when it was first encountered. Thinking it was a relatively small area of recent debris, part of the pit was removed. When it was realized that the pit was significantly larger than initially thought, it was recorded as a feature. While it was believed to be largely modern (for example, a plastic fork and modern lightbulb was recovered), we decided it would be better to risk uneccesarily recording a modern feature than risk not recording a historic feature. Debris observed in the pit seemed to be predominantly industrial or commercial debris, based on the relatively minimal amount of personal or domestic items. There was a large amount of architectual items, including brick and concrete fragments; sheet metal, piping, and other miscelanious metal fragments; and broken flat glass, as well as a light bulb. There were some domestic or personal items recovered, though, including bottles, ceramic dishware, and one plastic fork. This seems to be consistent with the area, where there are residences nearby but the immediate vicinity is a cabinet manufacturing business. While a variety of artifacts were encountered, only a handful were collected for dating purposes, so only diagnostic historic artifacts were pulled (Figure 22; Table 2). No sediments were collected for flotation. These included a vareity of glassware, ceramics, and plastic. The dates of these artifacts range throughout the twentieth century. Many of these could date as early of the 1930s, with the earliest end date in the 1950s up through the present. Historic aerial photography was not helpful in dating the trash pit, as the resolution was too low to see that sort of feature through the trees. Buildings were present in their current location by 1951, based off the earliest readily available aeiral images. A map of the county from the late 1930s does not show any buildings there, however. Assuming this is accurate, it indicates those structures were built during the the 1940s. Given date ranges for the material recovered, the debris could have been deposited there as early as the 1930s, but it is likely that the trash pit is associated with the structures in the mid-1900s. 49

Feature 5 Feature 5 was an amorphous stain encountered in the block excavation (Figure 23). The core of the feature was a reddish yellow (7.5YR 6/8) stain approximately 20 by 15 cm with the long axis going east-west. The outer portion of the feature was poorly defined dark brown (10YR 3/3) stain, approximately 95 by 40 cm with the long axis oriented northeast. Because this feature was discovered in the block excavation, Unit 1 was placed to capture Feature 5, but due to the diffuse nature of the feature boundary, it may have extended outside of the unit. When bisected, the feature faded and disappeared at about 10 cmbs at its deepest point (Figure 24). As the eastern half was exposed, the feature consisted of discontinuous orange stains that were also very thin. While a sample from the core of the feature was collected for flotation, it was not processed as this was determined to be a root burn. There were no artifacts recovered from this feature. 51

Feature 5 within Unit 1, bisected. Feature 6 Feature 6 was an amorphous stain encountered in the block excavation (Figure 25). There were two concentrations of charcoal stains, approximately 10 by 10 cm each. The outer portion of the feature was poorly defined very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) stain, approximately 80 by 80 cm. Because this feature was discovered in the block excavation, Unit 2 was placed to capture Feature 6, which was then bisected. Within the first level it was determined to likely be a root stain, as the diameter of the feature was reducing in size, indicative of a cone and not a basin shape (Figure 26). This was confirmed in the second level, as the location of the feature shifted. At the base of the second level, the stain had disappeared – excavation would have stopped except that a bone was found on the eastern side of the northern half. No other artifacts were found in the next level. As the southern half was being removed, the stain disappeared and charcoal stains consistent with a root burn were encountered, and excavation ceased (Figure 27). While a sample from the core of the feature was collected for flotation, it was not processed as this was determined to be a root burn. Charcoal was observed, and three small fragments of unidentifiable bone, weighing a total of 1 g, were collected. Unit 2 Unit 2 was opened up to excavate Feature 5, encountered in the block (see Figure 25). It was a 1-by-1-m unit excavated to a depth of 50 cmbd on the northern side or approximately 24 cmbs by hand. An arbitrary datum was established in the northeastern corner of the unit and hand excavations began at 26 cmbd (156.4 masl). 53

## Page 71

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Figure 27. Charcoal staining within Feature 6, Unit 2, Level 2. Feature 7 Feature 7 was a discontinuous, amorphous stain with FCR encountered in the block excavation (Figure 28). The feature stains included two dark brown (10YR 3/3) stains with FCR, one approximately 20 by 40 cm with the long axis oriented northwest and one approximately 20 by 30 cm with long axis oriented north-south, as well as a 20-by-30 reddish yellow (7.5YR 7/6) stain that is somewhat U-shaped with the longer axis oriented east-west. The boundaries of all these stains were indistinct. Because this feature was discovered in the block excavation, Unit 3 was placed to capture Feature 7. In the first level of the eastern side, it was determined to be a root stain because the feature shifted, and the tight cluster of charcoal was more in line with what would be expected from a root burn than a hearth (Figure 29). There was a small amount of FCR present, between 20 and 25 pieces under 5 cm (2 in) in size and seven that were between 5 and 10 cm (2 and 4 in) in size. It was hypothesized that these small rocks were burned in a natural fire rather than the result of cultural activity. Only one piece of FCR was collected. While a sample from the core of the feature was collected for flotation, it was not processed as this was determined to be a root burn. 55

Feature 8 connecting to Feature 4. Feature 9 Feature 9 was an amorphous stain encountered in the block excavation as it was exposed by the Gradall (Figure 34 and 35). The feature stain was a very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) stain with charcoal flakes and burned earth, approximately 100 by 50 cm, with the long axis oriented to the northwest; however, the feature was partially removed by the Gradall and that does not reflect its full extent. Because it was not a cultural feature, even though Unit 4 had been plotted, it was not excavated. While a sample from the core of the feature was collected for flotation, it was not processed as this was determined to be a root burn. 59

## Page 79

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 7: Faunal Analysis by Jeremy Loven Introduction Data recovery excavations conducted at 41EL2464 in Ellis County, Texas, yielded 559 faunal specimens. The faunal remains were collected during the mechanical excavation of Trench 6 and a 20-by-10-m block that were placed within the Waxahachie Creek floodplain. The collected faunal remains were predominantly found at elevations ranging from 154 to 158 cm below the modern ground surface. The remains were spread throughout the excavation block in a general southwestern to northeastern alignment, although a notable bone cluster was observed along the southern end of the block. The bones were interpreted as being in a secondary context and horizontally redistributed across the site as a result of alluvial activity (Short and Frederick 2019). All 559 specimens were analyzed and are addressed in this section (Table 3). Of these, 439 (78.53 percent) were identifiable to, at minimum, the level of size and class. The remaining 120 (21.47 percent) specimens were categorized as unidentified mammal or unidentified vertebrate. The vast majority of identifiable animal remains are mammalian in origin, with most being the bones of large-bodied mammals. The remaining specimens are reptiles (turtles and snakes). Additional animal remains were identified within a microartifact sample column placed in the eastern wall of the excavation block. Faunal specimens recovered from the sample column included the remains of rodents, fish, and turtles. These remains are documented further in Chapter 11 and not included in the bone counts provided in this section. Four bone samples were submitted for radiocarbon dating. These samples yielded three dates associated with the end of the Late Archaic Period (ca. A.D. 400 to 700) and one date from the thirteenth century or Late Prehistoric II Period. The dated bone, general depth of bone deposits and their association with flaked stone artifacts, and the presence of butchered bone with cut marks seemingly made by stone tools all suggest the faunal assemblage, particularly the large-mammal remains, were introduced by the prehistoric inhabitants of the site. Table 3. NISP and MNI values from 41EL246. ## Page 80

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Taxon Common Name/Size NISP MNI Very large mammal Bison and elk size 6 – Sylvilagus sp. Cottontail 1 1 Canis sp. Dog or coyote 1 1 Canidae Dog, coyote, fox 1 – Odocoileus sp. Deer 63 3 Medium artiodactyl Deer/pronghorn size 58 – Bison bison Bison 35 1 Bos/Bison Cow or bison 2 – Class Reptilia Testudinata indet. Turtle/tortoise 13 – Serpentes indet. Snake 1 – Total NISP/MNI 559 6 Analytical Methods and Assemblage Characteristics Identification Identifications of the 559 faunal specimens were made with the consultation of the analyst’s (Jeremy Loven) personal comparative collection, as well as published reference materials (Elbroch 2006; France 2009; Olsen 1960; Smart 2009). The initial step in the analysis of the animal remains involved identifying the particular skeletal element from which the specimens originated. Specimens (complete bones or bone fragments, or complete teeth or tooth fragments) in which the element could be identified were subsequently assigned to a particular taxonomic group. These groupings include the animal’s order, family, genus, or species. For specimens where the element could not be identified, an attempt was made to identify the size and class of animal that the specimen originated from, such as large or small mammal. All specimens in which neither the taxa nor a size and class determination could be made were categorized as unidentified vertebrate or unidentified mammal. For each identifiable specimen, an attempt was made to classify it to the lowest taxonomic level possible. During the analysis, each specimen was analyzed and identified on its own merit, and taxonomic identifications were determined by using diagnostic features present on the bones. Grouping and identifying by association was generally avoided. The grouping of bones together based solely on context and association can be quite problematic (Driver 2011) and is typically based on a level of assumption. In addition to identifying the element and taxon—or the size and class—of each identifiable specimen, numerous other attributes and modifications were recorded when possible including provenience, condition, portion, side, fusion, burning, artifact type, gnawing, natural modification, butchering marks, and total bone counts. For the purpose of identifying more discreet traits potentially present on the bones, such as gnaw marks and cut marks, a hand lens and/or magnifying lamp were used when necessary. 64

## Page 81

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Quantification For this analysis, the primary methods used for calculating the frequency of taxa within the assemblage is the Number of Identified Specimens (NISP) and the Minimum Number of Individuals (MNI). The NISP consists of a total count of all faunal remains comprising a faunal assemblage, with each element or fragment thereof counting as a single unit. Despite some flaws and problems with the NISP, it is the most widely utilized quantification method for calculating taxonomic abundance in current faunal analyses; all quantifications provided in this section are presented as NISP values unless otherwise indicated. Grayson (1984) clearly outlined the problems with the NISP. Significant issues with this method concern bone fragmentation and interdependence. Excessive bone fragmentation can potentially inflate NISP values, sometimes greatly. This can particularly impact large mammal specimens, whose “bones tend to be broken into more pieces, or more recognizable pieces, than those of smaller mammals, and thus overexaggerate the importance of larger mammals” (Driver 1985:11). To partially alleviate the problem of bone fragmentation on NISP values, bone fragments were refit when possible. NISP can also be influenced by the proportions of carcasses that enter a site and become part of the site’s archeological record. Leporids (rabbits and hares), rodents, and other small animals were more likely to be returned whole, in contrast to larger animals such as bison and deer where select bones and cuts of meat were generally brought back to the site, with lower utility elements left at the kill location. The MNI was calculated for specimens identified to the level of genus or species. It is the smallest number of individuals required to account for elements assigned to a particular taxon. Issues concerning MNI are defined in detail by Grayson (1984). MNI can often underestimate the total number of individual. Aggregation techniques can also greatly influence MNI values. For this study, all faunal material was treated as a single aggregate. Sampling Faunal specimens recovered during the data recovery excavations were primarily collected as encountered during mechanical trenching and stripping of the excavation block. Limited hand excavation and minimal use of fine-mesh screening undoubtedly has functioned to bias the assemblage in favor of large-bodied mammals, whose larger bones are more readily apparent than those of smaller animals. The bias is clear when comparing the results of the faunal analysis presented in this section with the faunal remains encountered in the microartifact sample column, which included fish and rodent remains. Fish bones and remains from smaller animals, particularly those weighing less than 340 g, or smaller than the size of a gray squirrel, are frequently underrepresented even when screening through ¼-in mesh (Schaffer 1992:131). As such, it is expected that the sample strategy employed during the current study would likely result in an underrepresentation of small animal remains and heavily fragmented bones. Fragmentation The animal remains comprising the 41EL246 faunal assemblage demonstrate a high degree of fragmentation, caused by both predepositional and postdepositional processes. In some instances, the substantial fragmentation of the faunal remains has made identification of the 65


---

## Page 82

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 skeletal elements quite difficult or impossible. Much of the predepositional fragmentation of bone is probably the result of marrow extraction activities. Bones from large mammals are commonly fractured in a variety of ways to obtain marrow (Binford 1981:148–162) and sometimes broken into smaller fragments to increase the rate of grease extraction from the bones (Church and Lyman 2003:1083), although evidence for grease extraction was generally absent. Postdepositional processes that affected bone fragmentation included natural taphonomic processes, such as weathering and erosion, and trampling and recovery damage caused during excavation activities. Versar lab personnel also noted many of the bones were in fragile condition, which resulted in increasing fragmentation during the cleaning and cataloguing process. Overall, 83 percent of the identified faunal specimens (including those identified to size-class categories) are less than one-quarter complete, while 92 percent of the specimens are less than one-half complete. Taxonomic Composition The 41EL246 faunal assemblage totals 559 specimens (see Table 3). Taxonomic groups represented within the assemblage are artiodactyls, leporids, canids, and reptiles; no fish, amphibians, or birds were identified. Mammals were the most ubiquitous fauna represented within the assemblage and account for 96.18 percent (n=425) of all identifiable remains, while reptiles made up 3.19 percent (n=14). Artiodactyls Artiodactyl remains documented in the assemblage totaled 158 specimens or 35.59 percent of all identified remains. An additional 50.11 percent of all specimens are classified as large mammal (n=187) or large to very large mammal (n=33), and these most likely originate from medium to large ungulates. Represented among the remains positively identified to the order Artiodactyla are specimens originating from indeterminate deer (Odocoileus sp.), deer or pronghorn (Antilocapra Americana), bison (Bison bison), and bison or cow (Bos taurus). Bison Prior to their extirpation in the late 1800s, bison inhabited much of the state of Texas (Schmidly and Bradley 1994), including lands around the current project area. Their prehistoric presence in north-central Texas also was documented archeologically at other sites in the region, including 41DL436 and 41HI1 (Jacobson 2015; Nickels 2015). Bison are represented in the assemblage by 35 specimens accounting for at minimum one individual. Most of the identifiable bison bone elements were vertebrae, rib, and mandible fragments, although one femur and one ulna fragment were also documented in the assemblage (Table 4). In addition to the remains identified as bison, five long bone shaft fragments and one rib fragment were categorized as “very large mammal.” These remains likely were bison in origin. 66


---

## Page 83

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Based on bison skeleton age criteria established by Koch (1935) and Duffield (1973), and the general size of the bison remains, the individual was likely 4 to 6 years old at the time of death; the proximal end of the humerus was fully fused while all the vertebral epiphyses remained unfused. Seasonality, or time of death, cannot be inferred from the bison remains. The bison skeletal elements at 41EL246 were predominantly high-utility (ribs, lumbar vertebrae, thoracic vertebrae) or middle-utility (femur, pelvis, cervical vertebrae). The only low-utility elements identified were the ulna and mandible fragments. The paucity of low-utility bison elements could indicate the kill was made a far distance from the camp site, resulting in primarily meat and high-utility elements being chosen for transport back to the site. Deer Deer were the most identified genera/species of animal within the 41EL246 faunal assemblage. The remains of these animals almost certainly included the white-tailed variety (Odocoileus virginianus) and possibly mule deer (Odocoileus hemionus). Sixty-three deer bones accounting for a minimum of three individuals were positively identified, as indicated by presence of three (right side) naviculo-cuboid tarsal bones. Based on the observance of epiphyseal fusion and bone size, the remains of at least one mature and one juvenile deer were brought back to the site. Skeletal element representation among deer was greater than that seen in bison. Elements from the forelimb and hindlimb (including the extremities) and axial skeleton were present, as were mandible and cranium fragments. The presence high-, medium-, and low-utility bones suggest that some or all of the deer carcasses were brought back to the site complete or near complete. In addition to the remains confidently identified as deer, 58 specimens were categorized as medium artiodactyl. These remains originated from a deer or pronghorn, with deer being most likely, as no pronghorn remains were positively identified within the assemblage. The 187 large mammal specimens also were likely deer in origin. Leporids Leporids were a common food source prehistorically but are extremely rare in the 41EL246 assemblage. Regional leporid types include species from the genus Lepus (jackrabbits) and the genus Sylvilagus (cottontails). The only leporid bone identified was a cottontail pelvis fragment. A small mammal long bone shaft fragment also was collected from the site, and this likely originated from a cottontail as well. The paucity of leporid and small mammal fragments at 41EL246 is potentially due to several factors, including local ecology, individual/ group behavior, and sample bias. Bison and deer were possibly present in the local environment in substantial numbers and obtainable within close proximity to the site, which could have made the animals a more attractive option for the site inhabitants, provided their large size and higher fat content than that of leporids and rodents. Additionally, the limited amount of fine screening conducted during the excavations almost certainly resulted in small animal remains being underrepresented in the faunal assemblage. 67

## Page 86

## Page 87

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Carnivores Carnivore specimens are extremely rare and are represented by only two bones. One of the bones was a femur fragment from the genus Canis (dog/coyote/wolf), and the other was a rib fragment identified to the level of family Canidae (dog/coyote/wolf/fox). The size of the bones suggests they are from a domestic dog or a coyote. Reptiles A total of 14 reptile specimens were recovered from 41EL246. The remains consist of 12 turtle/tortoise carapace fragments and 1 turtle/tortoise humerus fragment, along with one unidentified snake vertebra. Turtles and tortoises are commonly recovered from archeological sites and served as a food source. Additionally, turtle/tortoise carapaces and plastrons were used prehistorically as cups, dippers, and cooking vessels. Ethnographically, turtles are known to have been collected by prehistoric peoples in the Southwest for the purpose of using the turtle shells as dance rattles and gaming pieces (Beaglehole 1936:22; Parsons 1939:384–385). At 41EL246, it is indeterminate if the reptile specimens became incorporated into site sediments through cultural or natural processes. The reptile remains were all unburned and exhibited no evidence of cultural modification. As such, it is possible that they were washed into the site during postoccupational flooding events or were introduce to the site through other natural taphonomic processes. Modified Bone Burned and Butchered Bone The frequency of modified bone associated with cooking and butchering at 41EL246 is low (Table 5). A total of 3.76 percent (n=21) of all faunal remains exhibit evidence of burning/cooking or butchering. Evidence of modification was only observed on large mammals; no modification was observed on small- to medium-sized mammal or reptile remains. Cut marks from butchering/defleshing are present on 11 specimens, including remains identified as bison and deer (Figures 38 and 39). Fracturing caused by cultural processes was observed on four deer and two medium artiodactyl bones. Thermal alteration was definitively present on three bones: a bison rib, a medium artiodactyl metatarsal, and an unidentified mammal long bone shaft fragment. A radius fragment from a medium artiodactyl appeared to have been boiled. The bone was waxy and polished, and the fractured ends were partially blunted and rounded. Bone Artifacts Only one bone artifact was identified in the 41EL246 faunal assemblage. The artifact was a long bone shaft fragment fashioned into a scraper-like tool, with one margin ground to a sharp edge (Figure 40). Cut marks were present on the exterior portion of the bone. These were likely associated with initial defleshing and removal of meat from the bone and not the manufacturing of the tool. 71

## Page 90

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Figure 40. Bone tool created from a large mammal long bone shaft fragment. Summary and Conclusions Data recovery excavations at 41EL246 resulted in the recovery of a small faunal assemblage dominated by large-bodied mammals, notably deer and bison. Small mammal, canids, and reptiles also were identified within the assemblage; however, none of these remains exhibited evidence of cultural modification, and it is uncertain whether these remains entered the archeological record through cultural or natural processes. Regardless, the dominance of large mammal bones, comprising at minimum the remains of one bison and three deer, indicate that ungulates likely were the primary source of animal protein for the site inhabitants. 74

## Page 91

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 8: Aquatic and Land Mollusca at Site 41EL246 by Manuel R. Palacios-Fest, Ph.D. Summary Land and aquatic mollusks of the proposed reconstruction of US 77 in Waxahachie, Ellis County, Texas, shows a diverse and abundant assemblage valuable to reconstruct the paleoenvironmental history of the site during the previous human occupation. Site 41EL246 is located within the Blackland Prairies Region of north-central Texas. This ecotone consists of dark-colored, calcareous mollisoils. The Waxahachie Creek-1 site (41EL246) offers the unique opportunity to improve our understanding of the human-environment relationship in an archeological context. The prehistoric buried site falls within the floodplain south of Waxahachie Creek, containing lithic and faunal remains presumed to date between A.D. 700 and 1700. Versar submitted 63 flotation samples to Terra Nostra Earth Sciences Research, LLC (TNESR) to identify the mollusks present in the site. Due to time constraints, only a select number of samples were analyzed. These included the samples associated with Feature 1 and the lower six samples from the microartifact column. Samples from the microartifact column were quartered prior to analysis. Eighteen terrestrial gastropod species and twelve aquatic snails occurred in this location. Besides, nine bivalves (six mussels and three clams) occurred in the two sets of samples (Appendix A, Table A-1). One labeled F.S.-# (samples from the Feature 1 floatation, Appendix A, Table A-2); the other labeled WAX-# (samples from the microartifact column, Appendix A, Table A-3). The 57 F.S.-# samples were diverse and abundant with 14 land and 8 aquatic snails and 8 bivalves (five mussels, three clams); however, the WAX-# samples contained more land (18) and aquatic (12) species than the F.S.-# samples but only five bivalve species (two mussels, three clams). Table A-4 (in Appendix A) summarized the list of species identified herein and the ecological requirements of the species present in this investigation. The highly fragmented samples indicate most shells were reworked into a slightly reducing aquatic environment. Some shells were dark by reducing processes after burial. About 15 percent of the “complete” remains were not only broken but abraded. They were confirming the hypothesis of reworking. Overall, fragmentation and abrasion exceed 60 percent. Based on the preliminary analysis, it is fair to say that the land snails were mostly reworked from the slopes and ravines associated with Waxahachie Creek to the location of burial; however, not all specimens were allochthonous (originating at a distance from current position), the excellent preservation of several individuals from different species suggests they arrived settled and thrived in moist, riparian soil. By contrast, the aquatic forms show less fragmentation and abrasion but more reduction of the shells indicating the specimens buried rapidly under anoxic or dysoxic conditions. To date, limited geoarcheological and faunal data are available from the Blackland Prairie region of northcentral Texas. This pilot research may be a good and successful start of more detailed investigations to reconstruct past environments associated with human activity. 75

## Page 93

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 9: Lithic Analysis by Laura Short, Ph.D. Introduction Very few chipped stone artifacts were recovered from 41EL246. There was one chipped stone tool and six flakes recovered (Table 6). All the lithics were discovered near but not in direct context with bones (Figure 41). In addition, lithics were not recovered in the vicinity of bone fragments exhibiting cut marks (Figure 42). There was one flake 0 to 6.4 mm (0 to 0.25 in) in size, three flakes 6.4 to 12.7 mm (0.25 to 0.5 in) in size, one 12.7 to 19 mm (0.5 to 0.75 in) in size, and one 25 to 38.1 mm (1 to 1.5 in) in size. Only one flake retained its platform; however, because it was on the cortex, it was not possible to determine if it had been prepared at all. Three of the flakes were tertiary flakes with no cortex on their ventral side, while three were secondary flakes. Those three tertiary flakes were all from the same cluster of artifacts on the southern end of the block; two of them were likely from the same nodule, while the other appeared to be a different material entirely. These two flakes glow orange-red under ultraviolet light and are likely Edwards Chert. The rest of the flakes do not glow and are an unknown chert. While the number of flakes is not sufficient for a statistical analysis, it is notable that none of the flakes are complete, and only one retains its platform. This would be consistent with the tool manufacture pattern found by Sullivan and Rozen’s (1985) method for characterizing flake debitage assemblages, which are characterized by assemblages with very high percentages of flake fragments. The nature of the geomorphology, however, makes it impossible to know if separate clusters of artifacts are temporally related. Notably, the biface was not found in association with any of the flakes (see Figure 41). Table 6. Flakes recovered from 41EL246. ## Page 96

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 The chipped stone tool is an unbroken knife or thinned biface with a random flaking pattern (Figure 43). It is either an informal or unfinished form, as one side has a straight finished edge, and the other exhibits some sinuosity and lack refinement, falling between stage 3 and 4 on the TxDOT Chipped Stone Analytical Protocol. It has a maximum length of 55 m, maximum width of 29 mm, and a maximum thickness of 9 mm. It weighs 14 g. The material type is unknown, some sort of fine-grained silica, such as a chert. On the more refined side, there are some patches of reddish brown staining, possibly iron-oxide. As there was no observed use wear but the piece is not broken, it is not clear why it was discarded. The small size of the chipped lithic assemblage makes it impossible to make many observations regarding these activities on the site. It does confirm that people were engaging in lithic tool production or maintenance on the site. Figure 43. Chipped stone tool, FS 251. Fire-Cracked Rock Fire-cracked rock (FCR) is rock that has been heated, resulting in physical and chemical changes to the rock. People can create FCR as a byproduct of heating activities, especially in the preparation of food. The production of FCR requires high heat; however, it can also be produced in natural fires and therefore is not always an indication of human activity. FCR is identified by the physical changes, including changes in color and the presence of fracturing or crazing (which are incomplete fractures); soot and charcoal is sometimes observed on the surface of the rocks as well. Post-heating colors can range between reds, whites, and dark gray, depending on the mineral content of the rock and the temperature and duration of heating. FCR can be reused a limited number of times, as each time it is heated it breaks down. Eventually, the rock is too small to hold enough heat to be useful, and it is discarded. A total of 319 pieces of stone were recovered from Site 41EL246, excluding the heavy fraction of the flotation samples. An additional 206 stones from the flotation of Feature 1 were analyzed as well, for a total of 525 pieces, weighing 14.1 kilograms (kg). Stone was sorted by 80


![Image 1 - Page 96](assets/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery_p96_img1.png)


![Image 2 - Page 96](assets/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery_p96_img2.png)

## Page 97

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 size, weighed, and characteristics such as crazing and fractures were recorded. From the flotation samples, anything smaller than 0.5 in was sorted into FCR or not but not characterized, simply counted and weighed in bulk – this includes an additional 1,154 small stones, weighing 0.7 kg, of which 876 were determined to be FCR. Of the rest of the stones, 465 were determined to be FCR and 60 were determined to not be FCR. Not all stone encountered on site was recovered. During the first days of fieldwork, stones that were believed to be unburnt or FCR not associated with a feature were point plotted but not collected; however, due to the abundance of FCR unaffiliated with any kind of feature, it was decided to collect all the rock encountered. There was significant variation in color and form of the stones, including blocky, grey chunks of limestone; small, smooth, light-colored stones; and some dark red cobbles. In the lab, it was determined that many of stones that did not appear to be FCR in the field had actually been heated, so it likely that most of the uncollected stones were also FCR. In particular, many of the small, smooth, light-colored stones, which had been assumed to be river gravels, had orange to red centers indicative of heating. Stone were considered not FCR if they lacked fractures or crazing and did not exhibit a red or dark gray color. Without having the raw material, it is difficult to know what exactly a nonburnt stone would look like, but the Austin chalks are light-colored, starting gray and weathering to white. Limestone can turn either white or gray when heated, and it is common to develop a white rind with a darker internal color from heating (Duncan and Doleman 1991). The majority of the rock is limestone (n=507), while six are an unknown material, four are quartzite, three are calcite, two are likely chert, two are calcium carbonate nodules, and one is likely sandstone. Fifty-four (54) of the limestone fragments were determined to not be FCR, as well as the six pieces of unknown material. Of the limestone, there are four different kinds: one type has inclusions of shell and some sand, another has a large amount of sand in it but no shell, another is fine-grained with no apparent inclusions, and the final is a crystalline limestone. The unknown material reacts with hydrochloric acid and therefore is carbonate- based. It may not be limestone, however, as the pieces refit to create a cube shape with unnaturally straight sides, so this seems likely to have been human-made; this is also why it was not classified as FCR despite being fragmented. Additionally, it is possible that the sandstone is actually quartzite; that piece of FCR would not break but did produce sparks when hit with the hammer. All stones were sorted by size, using the categories of 0 to 0.5 in, 0.5 to 1 in, 1 to 1.5 in, 1.5 to 2 in, 2 to 3 in, 3 to 4 in, 4 to 6 in, and over 6 in (Figure 44 and Table 7). Excluded from this table are the over 1,100 stones smaller than 0.5 in from the flotation that were not fully analyzed. Notably, the FCR from nonflotation contexts has a bimodal pattern, while the FCR from flotation contexts and non-FCR were concentrated in the smaller size ranges. All the flotation samples are from Feature 1, which were observed in the field to have noticeably smaller stones than were present in the rest of the site. 81
