FCR from Size Range Total FCR from Nonflotation Total (inches) non-FCR Heavy Fraction Contexts 0–0.5 4 12 26 42 0.5–1 24 67 68 159 1–1.5 20 54 73 147 1.5–2 7 22 24 53 2–3 5 22 68 95 3–4 0 4 16 20 4–6 0 1 8 9 Total 60 182 283 525 Feature 1 was the only cluster of FCR that was determined to be the result of human activity, likely a single-use heating feature built by a small group of people. A total of 1,216 pieces of FCR were recovered Feature 1, including from the heavy fraction of the flotation. FCR recovered from Feature 1 included limestone, calcium carbonate nodules, and three pieces of calcite. The majority of the FCR was less than 0.5 in (n=910), followed by 0.5 to 1 in (n=107), and 1 to 1.5 in (n=95) (Figure 45). Various experimental studies have shown that stones smaller than 7.5 to 5 cm (3 to 2 in) are too small to be reused, representing the end of the lifecycle of FCR (Knapp 2015; Ng 2004). It is not anticipated that they would utilize stones that small for heating in an oven. Given the large number (n=88) of small smooth stones – what looked like water-worn pebbles – that exhibited signs of heating, it seems like the stones started at this small size. It has been suggested that a size-to-weight ratio can give insight to how the stones were used, as stone boiling creates blocky FCR while the slow heating in an oven creates curvilinear FCR (Duncan and Doleman 1991). Noncultural stone is used baseline for stone size-to-weight ratio; however, most of the stone from 41EL246 was burnt, and the only unburnt stones were very small. ## Page 99

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 flat (though the stones were not measured, these stones were approximately twice or more wide as they were tall), according to the size-to-weight ratio they are more blocky than the non- FCR stone, possibly indicating they were used for stone boiling (Figure 46). The smaller apparently starting size may also be more consistent with stone boiling than an oven function, though limestone is not an ideal stone for boiling food as it can leave grit as it breaks down. Though also not ideal, the stones could have been used to create a surface on which food was cooked directly. This might account for why smooth water-worn pebbles were used, since they might create as smooth a surface as possible if they area lacked larger flat stones. Another possibility is that this pavement does not represent a heating feature with the majority of the stones used a heating element; rather, they may have been used to create a raised or dry surface on which to build the fire. Regardless, these stones were unlikely to have been used more than once, and the small size of the feature indicates that if it was used to cook food, it would have not been able to cook for more than a few people, perhaps a small family or hunting party. Feature 1 FCR 60 11 1 32 95 107 910 0-0.5 in. 0.5-1 in. 1-1.5 in. 1.5-2 in. 2-3 in. 3-4 in. 4-6 in. Figure 45. Size distribution of fire-cracked rock from Feature 1. 83

## Page 100

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Size-to-weight Ratio Feature 1 200 180 160 140 ) g 120 ( t h 100 g ie W 80 60 40 20 0 0 1 0.52-1 in 1-13.5 in 1.54-2 in 2-53 in 3-64 in 4-67 in 8 Feature 1 FCR Non-FCR Figure 46. Size to weight ratio of fire-cracked rock from Feature 1. There is another cluster of FCR on the eastern side of the site. While from planview, it looks like a possible cooking feature, it is more likely to be a tree root burn (Figure 47). Along the northern side of this scatter, the profile to the south was already exposed, which was not consistent with an oven basin. After exposing the scatter in planview, it was bisected exposing the eastern profile, which shows at least two root burns (Figures 48 and 49). While excavating, it was noted that the FCR were not clast supported, as would be expected from an earth oven; this is shown in the profile of the FCR (Figure 50). There was a greater variation in stone types from this cluster including three pieces of quartzite, one piece of chert, and one piece of sandstone, in addition to 61 pieces of limestone. Notably, this also included the mysterious carbonate material that was cube-shaped when the pieces were refit. Compared to Feature 1, the FCR from this cluster was larger, with the 22 pieces in the 2-to-3-in range (Figure 51). Overall, however, these stones were relatively small for FCR used in earth ovens, with the bulk of the material being too small for reuse. The size-to-weight ratio is also blockier compared to Feature 1, with heavier weights for the same size stone (Figure 52). If the FCR were created in a cooking feature, they have since been heavily disturbed by the tree burn, possibly in a tree fall. 84

## Page 105

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 10: Five Flotation Samples from Site 41EL246, Ellis County, Texas by Leslie L. Bush, Ph.D. Five flotation light fractions from Site 41EL246, Ellis County, Texas, were submitted for identification prior to possible radiocarbon dating. Site 41EL246 is located on the floodplain of Waxahachie Creek in the Trinity River drainage. Cultural materials recovered and their position near the top of the West Fork soil (70 to 160 cmbs) suggest the site is a limited occupation campsite dating to the Late Prehistoric period (A.D. 700 to 1700) (Pemberton and Cliff 2008). The site lies within the modern city of Waxahachie. As of 2008, the area was covered by a mix of short grasses and a few pecan trees (THC Site form 1/29/2008). Ellis County is situated in the Northern Blackland Prairie ecoregion (32a) where historical vegetation was dominated by the tall grasses little bluestem (Schizachyrium scoparium), big bluestem (Andropogon gerardii), Indiangrass (Sorghastrum nutans), and tall dropseed (Sporobolus compositus), with switchgrass (Panicum virgatum) and eastern gamagrass (Tripsacum dactyloides) in moister areas (Omerick 1987; Omerick and Griffith 2014). Common trees along streams like Waxahachie Creek would have included bur oak (Quercus macrocarpa, white group), shumard oak (Q. shumardii, red group), hackberry (Celtis laevigata), elms (Ulmus spp.), ashes (Fraxinus spp.), eastern cottonwood (Populus deltoides), and pecan (Carya illinoinensis) (Omerick 1987). Methods Flotation light fractions were sorted according to standard techniques (Pearsall 2015). They were first weighed then placed on stack of graduated geologic mesh to facilitate sorting. Material that did not fall through the largest mesh (No. 10, 2-mm square openings) was examined under a Leica S9i stereoscopic microscope at 6-55X magnification. Uncarbonized plant material and nonbotanical materials (e.g., insects, soil peds) were removed, weighed, placed into a new plastic bag, and labeled. In these samples, no carbonized plant material larger than 2 mm was present. Material that fell through the 2-mm mesh was examined under the microscope, and smaller carbonized plant material was removed from these size fractions for identification and possible radiocarbon dating. All wood charcoal fragments recovered were too small to be snapped for clean transverse sections. Occasionally, a transverse section was fortuitously present and could be examined (Figure 53). In most cases, wood charcoal identification was made using aspects of wood anatomy visible in longitudinal sections, notably the presence of vessels, fibers, and rays wider than uniseriate. After identification, specimens were labeled, recorded, and weighed on an Ohaus Scout II 200 x 0.01 g electronic balance. To retain suitability for radiometric dating, samples were placed on freshly washed glassware when not in specimen containers, and they were handled only with vinyl gloves, small paintbrushes, and metal forceps. After identification, specimens were placed in a new microcentrifuge tube. 89

## Page 106

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Botanical materials were identified to the lowest possible taxonomic level by comparison to materials in the Macrobotanical Analysis comparative collection and through the use of standard reference works (e.g., Hoadley 1990; InsideWood 2004; Panshin and de Zeeuw 1980; Wheeler 2011). Plant nomenclature follows that of the PLANTS Database (USDA, NCRS 2020). Figure 53. Transverse section of hardwood (angiosperm) charcoal in from FS #325, 41EL246 (photographed in clean sand). Results Plant materials present in the flotation samples are shown in Table 8. Uncarbonized plant material is composed of weedy and woody taxa consistent with plants in the site area today. It is interpreted as modern. Carbonized plant parts consisted entirely of wood charcoal fragments that fell through the 2-mm mesh. From such small fragments, the woods present could be identified only as hardwoods (angiosperms). In central Texas, native woody angiosperms consist of trees and shrubs other than junipers (Juniperus spp.), pines (Pinus spp), and baldcpress (Taxodium distichum), which are gymnosperms. One specimen that would be examined in transverse section is consistent with members of the legume family (Fabaceae; see Figure 53) including mesquite (Prosopis glandulosa). Identification from a section less than half a millimeter across is not certain, however. If the looser texture in the upper right of the image is not interpreted as parenchyma, characteristic of many legume woods, the specimen would be consistent with mulberry (Morus spp.). Wood charcoal fragments recovered are illustrated in Figures 54 through 57. No charcoal of any kind was recovered from FS 58. 90

## Page 111

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 11: Geoarcheological Analysis by Charles D. Frederick and August Costa Introduction The excavations at 41EL246 took place on the floodplain of Waxahachie Creek. The section that follows describes the geomorphology, stratigraphy, and context of cultural material observed within the excavation block. Geology Waxahachie Creek is a tributary of the Trinity River, and nearly the entire drainage basin upstream of the site is underlain by the upper Cretaceous age Austin formation (also known as the Austin Chalk; Bureau of Economic Geology 1987). The western/upstream drainage divide between Mountain Creek and Waxahachie Creek corresponds with the contact between the Eagle Ford Shale and the Austin Chalk, and forms the White Rock or Austin Chalk escarpment (also known as the White Rock Cuesta; Read 1957). The bedrock in the immediate vicinity of the site is identified by Allen (1975:13–14) as the Middle Austin Chalk which is described as 140 ft of alternating chalky marl beds 1 to 2 ft thick and thicker (2 to 4 ft thick) beds of calcareous shale. The chalky marl beds form tan- to buff-colored, resistant ledges in outcrop exposures, and the softer marls are bluish gray and easily disintegrate upon weathering. Thin beds (1 to 2 in) of bentonite occur within the middle Austin Chalk, as do pyrite concretions. Holocene alluvium has been sporadically mapped along the creek by the Bureau of Economic Geology (1987), but examination of the geomorphology of the valley and the site deposits (which they mapped as bedrock) demonstrate that this is a gross underestimation of the late Quaternary alluvium. More accurate maps can be found on the distribution of the Trinity Clay in the 1911 soil maps provided by Bennett et al. (1911), and the geologic maps of Peabody (1961), Read (1957, just upstream of Waxahachie) and Allen (1975:Plate 1). Several of these show that the site lies within a narrow section of the valley, presumably constrained by slightly harder, perhaps chalkier bedrock. The constriction in the valley is flanked by much wider sections immediately upstream and downstream. Today, the soils at the site are mapped as the Frio silty clay (0 to 1 percent, frequently flooded) (Brooks et al. 1964) which are classified as fine, smectitic, thermic Cumulic Haplustolls. Described as “very deep, well drained, moderately slowly permeable soils that formed in calcareous loamy and clayey alluvium” (National Cooperative Soil Survey 2020), the typical pedon of the Frio series exhibits an A1-A2-A3-A4-Bk profile where the calcic horizon lies between 1 and 2 m below the surface. The most detailed study of the late Quaternary deposits of the creek were done downstream of Waxahachie in association with the development of Bardwell Lake by Slaughter and Thurmond (1965). They recognized three aggradational units associated with Waxahachie Creek: 1) a floodplain, 2) a first terrace, and 3) a second terrace. The floodplain (T-0) is described as underlain by mostly black to brown loamy clay containing occasional gravel lenses. The first terrace (T-1) forms a bench the tread of which lies 20 to 25 ft above the 95


---

## Page 112

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 floodplain and is underlain by light yellowish brown (5YR 6/4 to 10Y 6/2) silty clay. The second terrace (T-2) was noted to stand 65 ft above the floodplain and comprise 10 ft of grayish orange clayey sand over 40 ft of interbedded grayish orange to light brownish gray sandy clay and clayey gravel. Calcium carbonate nodules are noted to be present throughout though not abundant. Both the first and second terraces were presumed to be of Pleistocene age. Read (1957), who mapped the geology of the Midlothian Quadrangle immediately upstream of Waxahachie, described two terraces of Waxahachie Creek which he named Alluvium 1 and Alluvium 2. Alluvium 1 is associated with a terrace that stands more than 50 ft above the floodplain and is described as comprised of a matrix of yellow clay. Although the height of this surface places this terrace between Slaughter and Thurmond’s (1965) first and second terraces, the brief color description more closely resembles their first terrace. Alluvium 2, although not explicitly stated as occupying the floodplain, is based on his description of “dark brown to black clay with lenses of fresh chalk fragments which are up to boulder size” (Read 1957:109) presumed to be the same as Slaughter and Thurmond’s floodplain unit. No detailed description or study of the modern floodplain of Waxahachie Creek beyond soil survey descriptions and the brief synopses noted above was found in the literature. Geomorphology The block excavation was situated on the floodplain of Waxahachie Creek a short distance south of the present channel. The floodplain surface lies about 10 m above the thalweg of the creek. A low-relief linear depression crossed the block from the southwest to the northeast and dropped in elevation about 1.5 m in this distance (Figure 58). This feature clearly drained into the creek and appears to once have been blocked off by a bank of earth immediately north of the block excavation, but this berm was subsequently breached. This linear depression was noted by Pemberton and Cliff (2008:16) to have originally been thought to be a vestige of a former creek channel but may be a recent artificial drainage channel. A large mound, in part obscured by dense vegetation, was situated at the northwestern corner of the block, on the left bank of the linear depression and the right bank of Waxahachie Creek. Subsequent excavation revealed the toe of this mound to be comprised of modern fill (see Column Profile, Appendix B). Examination of Lidar digital terrain model (see Figure 58) reveals the presence of a low-relief levee immediately adjacent to the channel in some places along the right bank of the creek both upstream and downstream of the site. 96


---

## Page 113

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Figure 58. Map derived from a lidar digital terrain model showing the location of the block excavation with respect the the topography of the Waxahachie Creek floodplain. Note the linear depression that traverses the block. Field Methods Initially, the scope of work called for the excavation of a single trench along the western side of the block, from which the stratigraphy would be assessed and then excavation of the block would follow. It became immediately apparent that a 3-m deep, single-bucket-wide trench would not be safe, so this initial trench was then benched on two sides. Part of this trench (Trench 6) was described and drawn (Figure 59), but between rain and competing schedules, only part of this trench was recorded in detail. It was then decided that the site stratigraphy would be worked out from the eastern wall of the block excavation. Like Trench 6, the eastern wall was benched about halfway down. Each vertical face was cleaned with a trowel and major stratigraphic interfaces scored. These two faces were then photographed in order to compile a SfM three-dimensional scaled image (Figure 60). A hand drawn profile was also compiled (Figure 61), and of the two images, the latter is the more complete stratigraphic record. Descriptions of the deposits observed in the eastern wall profile, the partial profile of Trench 6, and a column immediately above Feature 1 in the northwestern corner of the block are presented in Appendix B. 97

## Page 116

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Sediment/soil samples were collected from the eastern wall of the block excavation to provide a detailed view of changes in sedimentation through time and where within this sequence of deposits human habitation occurred. Once the internal structure of the deposits exposed along the eastern wall became clear, it was obvious that the dipping strata would preclude any single-column sample from adequately capturing the all of the deposits exposed by the block excavation, but the paucity of cultural deposits did not seem to warrant more than one column sample. Eventually three sample suites were collected: 1) a continuous 50-by-50-cm column collected in 5-cm levels for microartifact analysis; 2) a nearly continuous column sample of bulk sediment immediately adjacent to the microartifact column; and 3) a grab sample from every context identified on the eastern wall profile. Bulk sediment samples aside from the microartifact column were collected in 2.5-cm plastic paleomagnetic cubes. Lab Methods Overall, 121 samples were characterized in the lab for particle size, magnetic susceptibility, calcium carbonate content, and organic carbon and stable carbon isotopes. The microartifact column comprised 54 samples, and an additional 10 samples from Feature 1 were analyzed for magnetic susceptibility. Much of the data generated by these analyses are presented in Appendix C and shown graphically on Figure 62. Bulk Sediment Analysis The bulk samples collected from the site were first weighed and then measured for the magnetic susceptibility on a Bartington MS2 meter and a MS2b sensor in SI units on the 0.01 sensitivity setting. From these data, the mass corrected low frequency magnetic susceptibility (X ) and the coefficient of frequency dependence (X ) were calculated. Then the color was lf fd determined using an X-Rite Capsure color matching tool. Then samples were split for particle size analysis and organic carbon assay. The particle size analysis was performed on a Beckman-Coulter LS 13 320 multiwavelength laser particle size analyzer, after pretreatment with a 5 percent solution of sodium hexametaphosphate to disperse the clays, and subsequent boiling in sodium hypochlorite (bleach) on a hotplate for approximately 20 minutes to destroy organic matter that might bind together mineral grains. From this analysis the percentages of sand, silt, and clay for the <2-mm size fraction was determined, as well as various descriptive statistics of the particle size distribution such as the mean particle size, median, sorting (standard deviation), skewness, and kurtosis. Once the particle size analysis was completed, the samples were oven-dried at low temperature (105˚C) and then a small split (~2 g) of the <2-mm matrix of each sample was ground in a mortar and pestle, and then used to determine the calcium carbonate equivalent (CCE) by means of a Chittick apparatus (Dreimanis 1962; Machette 1986). Carbon Content and Stable Carbon Isotopes Small (~1 g) splits of the bulk samples were submitted to Bruce Barnett at the Keck Paleoenvironmental & Environmental Stable Isotope Laboratory at the University of Kansas for the determination of the organic carbon and nitrogen content and the stable carbon and nitrogen composition of the organic matter. These samples were decalcified before analysis, dried and then weighed, pressed into tin capsules, and combusted in a Costech Instruments 100


---

## Page 117

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 ECS 4010 (Elemental Combustion System) that was interfaced with a Thermo Finnigan MAT 253 Stable Isotope Mass Spectrometer. For each sample, the percent carbon and nitrogen was determined, as well as the carbon:nitrogen ratio and stable isotopic composition of carbon and nitrogen (13C and 15N). Figure 62. Top Panel: Plot of the results of the analytical work from bulk sediment samples collected adjacent to the microartifact column. Heavy lines on the mean particle size, organic carbon and stable carbon isotopes are 3-point running averages. Bottom Panel: Plot of counts of selected materials identified in the microartifact samples shown with respect to the magnetic susceptibility. Tones project the stratigraphic position of historic (blue) and Late Prehistoric (pink) occupations identified on the basis of materials observed in the microartifact column. Microartifacts The large samples collected for microartifact analysis were first split into two 5-gallon buckets to which approximately a cup of baking soda was added and then filled with water and gently agitated with a power drill-operated stirrer. After sitting for a while, the samples were then wet sieved through a 1-mm stainless steel sieve, and the residue subsequently dried in an oven. Once dry, the samples were then passed through 4- and 2-mm sieves and then sorted under a microscope. The results of the microartifact analysis are presented in tabular form in Appendix D. The microartifact samples yielded an enormous quantity of snails and 101

Unfortunately, this work could not be done in time allotted and was terminated prior to completion. The incomplete analysis is presented in Chapter 8. Radiocarbon Dating The age of the deposits sampled by the microartifact column/bulk sample column on the eastern wall was established by the dating of five samples of charcoal picked from the microartifact assemblage. Samples were submitted to Beta Analytic for AMS dating and are presented in Table 9. Analysis of the dating results was done using the Bchron package in R studio, the results of which are discussed in detail, below. Site Observations Age of the deposits Given the geometry of the deposits revealed by the eastern wall profile (see Figure 61), the oldest sediments exposed by the block excavation were located at the base of the northern wall, where Feature 1 was situated, and the youngest deposits were exposed near the surface on the southern side of the block and were of Historic age. A charcoal sample collected from Feature 1 returned a conventional radiocarbon age of 2,440±30 BP (Beta-577332; 2,700 to 2,357 cal BP; 751 to 408 cal B.C.). The age of the sediments sampled by the microartifact column on the eastern wall was determined by five radiocarbon dates on charcoal picked from the microartifact residues, and these ages ranged from 1,180 radiocarbon BP near the base of the column to 460 radiocarbon BP at about an 80-cm depth (see Table 9). To better understand the temporal structure of the column deposits, an age-depth model was produced using the Bchron package in R studio (Parnell 2014; R Development Core Team 2014). Bchron, short for Bayesian chronology, takes the conventional radiocarbon ages, laboratory errors, and depths to calculate an age-depth model that first calibrates the dates, and then uses a Compound Poisson-Gamma distribution to arrive at a linear piece-wise age- depth chronology using Bayesian principles (Parnell et al. 2015; Parnell et al. 2008; Haslett and Parnell 2008). The results are output as a graphic plot (Figure 62) that shows the mean age at each depth as well as 2-sigma (95 percent) confidence interval and the radiocarbon probability distribution for each age used in the model. Examination of the model suggests that these deposits aggraded at a rate of 1 cm every 3 to 6 years (Figure 63). Although the age-depth model appears fairly linear, plotting the sedimentation rate every 5 cm reveals two prominent periods of more rapid aggradation, between 95 and 125 cm (398 to 518 cal BP) when the rate was an average of 0.3 cm/year and between 170 and 205 cm (728 to 845 cal BP) when it was aggrading 0.26 cm/year. The rest of the profile the rate of deposition was relatively steady between 0.21 and 0.23 cm/year. Overall, this sedimentation rate is what Ferring (1986) categorized as a moderate. 102

## Page 120

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Figure 63. Stratigraphy As noted previously, stratigraphic observations were made in three places within the block, the most comprehensive of which was the eastern wall profile of the block excavation, which recorded a complete north-south stratigraphic section (see Figures 60 and 61). An incomplete profile was made of the upper vertical face of the eastern wall of Trench 6 (see Figure 59), and a single-column profile was made in the northwestern corner of the block excavation adjacent to Feature 1. All of the samples collected from the site for characterization of the deposits were derived from the eastern wall of the block excavation and it from these data that the stratigraphy is described and interpreted. Deposits Exposed on the Eastern Wall Profile The site deposits, although at first appeared to be massive, were reasonably well-bedded, but the subtle stratigraphic variation was rather challenging to observe. The rough-cut, vertical faces made by the Gradall generally failed to reveal useful stratigraphic evidence, but once these cuts were cleaned with a trowel, the subtle stratigraphy could be observed if the lighting conditions were favorable. Bright sunlight hampered their visibility, and the most useful observations were made in the diffuse early morning light, often associated with an overcast sky. The eastern wall profile, which was separated into two vertical faces separated by a horizontal bench, was cleaned and annotated from the north to the south, as the final stages of excavation concluded. The structure of the deposits was gradually revealed as the wall was 104


![Image 1 - Page 120](assets/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery_p120_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 120*


---

## Page 121

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 cleaned, and prominent interfaces were scored with a trowel. The rendition of the eastern wall stratigraphy shown on the annotated SfM (see Figure 60) reflects the clear interfaces that were scored while cleaning the wall, but this figure does not completely capture the stratigraphy. Figure 61 presents a more nuanced interpretation of the stratigraphy that sought to resolve the stratigraphic ambiguities visible on Figure 60. There are still a few interfaces that could not be traced, and they were left as enigmatic, unresolved features. Twenty-nine strata were identified on the eastern wall profile, some of which were subdivided. These sedimentary deposits ranged from thin to thick beds (~10 to 40 cm) of black (5Y2.5/1, 2.5YR 2.5/, 7.5YR 2.5/1, all moist colors), very dark gray (7.5YR 3/1), very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2), dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2, 2.5Y 4/2), and dark brown (7.5YR 3/2), fine- grained alluvium ranging in texture from clay to silty clay, silty clay loam, and silt loam. A few of the strata had thin laminations of sand which were noticeably lighter in color, typically pale brown (10YR 6/3). The interfaces between beds ranged from sharp to gradual and, in some instances, became untraceable. Near the surface some of the deposits presented a weak prismatic but the vast majority of the deposits exhibited subangular blocky and occasionally angular blocky structure. Pedofeatures were generally uncommon, although a few strata exhibited patchy iron-manganese coats, pressure faces, and ped coats of iron or, in some cases, clay. Once the profile was cleaned, it was possible to detect subtle variations of alternating lighter colored and darker colored beds (see Figure 60), at least two of which (Zones 16 and 23) were thought in the field to possibly be soil A-horizons, but it was clear that distinguishing between dark-colored fine-grained muds and dark-colored, pedogenically altered sediments (A-horizons) was impossible in the field. Coarser-textured sediments exhibit slightly higher Munsell values, and this is most likely an artifact of the color of limestone sand and silt. It was thought in the field that color variations, specifically the Munsell value, may reflect differences in organic matter content, but there is a poor inverse correlation between these two attributes (r=-0.417 dry; r=-0.368 moist). A visual example of this is shown on Figure 64, where the greatest amount of organic carbon in the profile occurs in a lighter colored bed (Zone 16b). The absence of clear evidence of pedogenic alteration resulted in most deposits being recorded as C horizons, and it was hoped that laboratory analysis of the sediments would clarify whether there was significant pedogenic alteration of the deposits. In the lab, it became apparent upon drying that many of these deposits exhibited calcium carbonate filaments (an incipient calcic horizon), but these features were not generally observed in the field owing to the very moist state of the deposits. All of the deposits reacted strongly or violently with hydrochloric acid, and this reflects the considerable calcium carbonate content which ranged between ~40 and 80 percent. 105

## Page 123

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Patterns in the Bulk Sediment Analytical Data Upon completion of the lab work, the data were examined to see if there was any evidence of pedogenic alteration of the deposits that would support the field interpretations. The data exhibit patterned variation with depth, but many of these trends do not respect the stratigraphy that was visible in the field. For instance, the organic carbon exhibits two peaks, one at the modern ground surface and another around 90 cm, below which values decrease fairly steadily with depth. The calcium carbonate exhibits a striking stepwise increase with depth, with relatively low values in historic age deposits, moderate values in the middle of the profile, and the highest values in the bottom third of the section. There was no clear reflection of this pattern observed in the field. The magnetic susceptibility exhibits three major peaks, one in the historic age deposits, another around 145 cm depth, and a third at 250 cm. And like the organic matter, there is no clear relationship with the stratigraphy. Where the microartifact and cube column samples were collected, the vertical hatch on the stratigraphy column of Figure 62 is intended to convey which of these beds were relatively darker colored. Zones 1 through 8, 16, and 23 appeared in the field to be slightly darker colored than the others and were thought to possibly be soil A-horizons. To test this hypothesis, it was anticipated that soil A-horizons would differ from dark-colored muds by elevated organic carbon, elevated magnetic susceptibility, and a decrease in calcium carbonate. Zones 1 through 8, a suite of sediments that have been deposited during the Historic period (estimated to be the last 200 years), exhibit properties consistent with pedogenic alteration (elevated organic carbon which increases to the modern ground surface, elevated magnetic susceptibility (some of which may be attributable to metal in these deposits), and a decrease in calcium carbonate. The pattern of calcium carbonate is, however, a bit different from what one may expect from leaching during pedogenesis where the greatest depletion is at the modern ground surface. Hence, the trend in carbonate may be more an artifact of lithology/texture than pedogenesis. Zone 16, which traverses the entire eastern wall profile (Figure 61), exhibits a small, magnetic susceptibility rise and a subtle decrease in calcium carbonate but is only part of the major increase in organic carbon recorded in this profile, but the main peak in organic carbon was centered on the lighter-colored bed beneath it, which exhibits a decrease in magnetic susceptibility and a rise in calcium carbonate (see also Figure 64). Zone 23, which parallels Zone 16 but about 1 m lower, was notably darker colored in the field yet exhibited similar carbon content to the bounding beds. Zone 23 and the upper part of Zone 24 exhibited a minor increase in magnetic susceptibility and a slight decrease in calcium carbonate. Considered together, none of these darker, possible A-horizons exhibited all of the attributes typically associated with soils. Indeed, there is no clear relationship between organic carbon content and color or texture. Some of this may be due to the relative youth of the deposits, as any pedogenic alteration can reasonably be expected to be minimal for deposits this young. 107


---

## Page 124

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Another factor that might explain the depth patterns in organic carbon content is the sedimentation rate, as soil formation is typically inversely related to it. Pedogenic processes dominate when sedimentation rate is slow and diminish or cease when the sedimentation rate is high. Examination of the column stratigraphy, organic carbon content versus depth curve with respect to proxy evidence of flooding (limestone gravel identified in the microartifact column; and the percentage sand), and the apparent sedimentation rate calculated every 5 cm from the Bchron age-depth model (see Figure 63) fails to reveal a clear relationship. The main peak in organic carbon at 95 cm occurs during a period of more rapid sedimentation, whereas the expectation for the development of a soil A-horizon would be for it to coincide with a period of slower sedimentation. If anything, the age-depth model suggests that deposition here was relatively consistent through time, and the carbon content is not due to a period of slower sedimentation rate. Alluvial Architecture Cleaning of the eastern wall profile revealed that these deposits were not massive flood basin sediments but rather reasonably well-bedded sediments more typical of a channel fill or near channel depositional environment. The internal geometry of the deposits seen on Figure 61 resembles a levee, with elevated ground adjacent to the present Waxahachie Creek channel, and beds that dip away from the channel. As the deposit aggraded, sediment built upwards as well as outwards to the south. Sedimentation during the Historic period appears to have eliminated most of the difference in relief that existed adjacent to the channel and on the floodplain to the south. Although a levee is the first and most obvious interpretation of these deposits, it is possible that these deposits may also be the fill of a paleochannel or cutoff channel. Unfortunately, the absence of an east-west profile leaves us with an incomplete data set from which to assess this hypothesis. During the initial phase of excavation, the linear depression that traversed the block was thought to possibly be a vestige of a paleochannel, but the early excavation profiles indicated that the bedding within the deposits failed to respect this topographic feature, and on this basis, the hypothesis was rejected, and the linear depression interpreted as a modern drainage feature. Long after the excavation was complete, the three- dimensional point cloud of items (bone, FCR, etc.), recorded with the total data station during the excavation, was examined, and it appears that there is a westward dip to some of these items that may indicate that the sediments not only dip from north to south but also from east to west, as if the surficial drainage feature was superimposed upon an ancient paleochannel (Figure 65). There are few good examples of such paleochannel fills associated with prehistoric occupation, but one of the best was reported from the Gemma Site (Ferring and Yates 1987:51–100) at Lake Ray Roberts. The deposits of Unit 3, the older of two paleochannel fills recognized at the site, contained multiple flood drapes that were deposited within an abandoned channel resulting in a succession of 10- to 40-cm-thick dipping beds that sealed multiple Late Archaic occupations. Evidence from the eastern profile reveals that there was higher ground on the northern side and lower ground to the south, but whether the lower ground was merely the floodplain or 108


---

## Page 125

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 some sort of relict channel depression is impossible to be certain of from our stratigraphic window. Figure 65. Model showing surficial drainage feature superimposed upon an ancient paleochannel. Cultural Material within the Deposits and Analytical Units Geoarcheological assessment of the cultural inclusions within the deposits was primarily accomplished through analysis of the microartifact content of the 50-by-50-cm continuous column sample collected in 5-cm levels from the eastern wall profile. The magnetic susceptibility data obtained from the bulk sample column situated adjacent to the microartifact column contributed tangentially to this analysis. From these data, the stratigraphic position of Historic and prehistoric cultural material is quite clear, and approximately five occupation levels can be identified on the basis of the microartifact assemblage (Table 10). Historic debris was constrained to the top 45 cm of the column, where slag, coal, metal, and concrete were the most tangible evidence. Also present with these deposits were several deer bones, some of which had been percussed or gnawed upon by rodents. Evidence of multiple prehistoric occupations was found in the middle portion of the column, between 110 and 210 cm deep, and was indicated by the presence of debitage, bone, burnt bone, burned shell, and charcoal. Both bone and charcoal occurred in 109


![Image 1 - Page 125](assets/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery/00 Short et al 2021 - Waxahatchie Creek 41EL246 Data Recovery_p125_img1.png)

## Page 126

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 strata lacking any evidence of occupation as well. Although the precise positions of these low- density occupations are somewhat debatable in part depending upon one’s tendency to be a lumper or a splitter, in aggrading settings such as this it is likely that splitting is the most appropriate approach. With this in mind, four probable prehistoric occupations are identified within the column deposits, all of which are of Late Prehistoric age. The shallowest of these, Occupation 2, lies between 115 and 130 cmbs and is estimated, on the basis of the Bchron age-depth model, to date to approximately 481 to 542 cal BP. It contained a piece of debitage, some burned shell and burned bone, and an assortment of small fauna (rodent, unidentified small mammals, and fish). Occupation 3 occurs between 145 and 160 cmbs and is estimated to date to 614 to 688 cal BP. This occupation contained two flakes and burned shell in addition to fragments of rodents, small unidentified mammals, and fish. This occupation is also associated with a prominent peak in the magnetic susceptibility that is not attributable to pedogenic processes, suggesting that there is likely a hearth or other thermal feature nearby. Between 170 and 190 cm, the microartifact column yielded one flake, seven pieces of microdebitage, burned shell, and fragments of rodent, reptile, turtle, and fish remains. This occupation (Occupation 4) was also accompanied by a significant peak in charcoal and is estimated to date to 728 to 805 cal BP. The lowest presumed occupation, 5, is the only one lacking durable artifacts, but the presence of burned shell and burned bone is consistent with the other occupations, all of which yielded debitage. Occurring between 200 and 210 cmbs, this occupation is estimated to date to approximately 845 to 903 cal BP. Not present in the microartifact column is one other known occupation, and that is the one associated with Feature 1. This hearth (Figure 66) was found at a depth of approximately 2.8 to 2.9 m below the surface in the northwestern corner of the block excavation and is almost 1,400 cal years older than the lowest occupation noted in the microartifact column. The dramatic age difference hints at either a stratigraphic discontinuity between these two locations, a dramatically different depositional regime, or a date on old wood. When first uncovered, there was some discussion of whether this apparent feature was indeed an in situ thermal installation. In order to assess this horizontal transect of magnetic susceptibility, samples was collected across the feature, with the expectation that if this feature was intact, it would exhibit a magnetic susceptibility increase within the feature and a corresponding decrease away from it. As can be seen on Figure 66, that is precisely what analysis of the magnetic susceptibility samples reveals. 110


---

## Page 127

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Figure 66. Top: line chart showing how the magnetic susceptibility varies along a horizontal transect across Feature 1. Bottom: Drawing of Feature 1 (by Joe Sanchez) showing the location of the magnetic susceptibility samples with respect to the feature. Considering all of this information together, it seems likely that there are only three functional analytical units: 1) the historic material (Occupation 1), 2) all of the Late Prehistoric occupations in the column lumped into one unit (Occupations 2, 3, 4 and 5), and 3) the material in proximity to Feature 1. The stratigraphic position of these units have been projected onto Figure 61 using different colored fills. The position of the Late Archaic Analytical Unit being most speculative as Feature 1 was located on the opposite side of the block excavation from this profile. If the site deposits were flat-lying, then it might be possible to easily separate the four prehistoric occupations in the column into at least two groups, but the complex geometry of the deposits greatly complicates teasing out individual artifacts and assigning them into occupational groups given that elevation alone has no consistent age significance. Discussion Analysis of the stratigraphy, attributes of the sedimentary deposits, the cultural components within the microartifact column, and the age of the deposits reveals that the block excavation sampled either a channel or near channel depositional environment that contained evidence of human occupation during three periods: Historic, Late Prehistoric, and Late Archaic. The 111

Most of the small items recovered from the high stratigraphic resolution (5-cm) column sample would most likely not have been seen on the intermittent, irregular scraped surfaces exposed by the Gradall. Projecting the evidence of human habitation in the column sample out into the block excavation is complicated by highly variable three-dimensional geometry of the alluvial deposits, which appear to dip both north to south and east to west. ## Page 129

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 The alluvial deposits exposed by the excavation exhibited very subtle color and texture variations which permitted identification of the internal geometry of the deposits, but failure to record these deposits on all four excavation walls (which was never part of the scope of work) resulted in incomplete stratigraphic record of the site deposits. The alternating light- and dark-colored sediments do not appear to represent soils and pedogenically unaltered sediments but rather natural variation in the color of the alluvial deposits which is to some extent predicated by the texture, with coarser-textured sediments lighter-colored and finer- textured ones darker. This color variation appears to bear little correlation with the organic carbon content of the sediments, and few of the deposits exhibit physical attributes that are consistent with the formation of soil A-horizons. Vegetation Change Through Time: The Carbon Isotopic Record The stable carbon isotopic record derived from analysis of the organic carbon suggests that prior to about 450 cal BP the valley floor was most likely either a grassland, or a savannah- like environment, with the carbon in the deposits being derived from between 50 and 60 percent C organic matter. In the last 500 years, there have been three cycles of more C -rich 4 3 organic matter, presumably reflecting increasingly forested conditions in the riparian corridor adjacent to the channel. The most pronounced and first of these is accompanied by a shift in carbon isotopes from approximately -19 to -20 per mil (~50 to 60 percent C biomass) to 4 about -24 per mil (~20 to 25 percent C biomass) reflects a significant increase in organic 4 matter contributed by C plants, most likely trees, that occurred between ~300 and 500 years 3 ago. Subsequent phases of more C -rich organic matter occurred around 200 years ago and 3 recently. This evidence implies that the riparian ecosystem that Jurney et al. (1990:2–8) argue was present at the time of European settlement, specifically riparian forests comprised of elm, hackberry, Spanish oak, pecan, red haw, walnut, mesquite, and mulberry, is a recent attribute of this environment. Stratigraphic Correlations with Trinity River Sequence In general terms, the deposits exposed by the block excavation are temporally consistent with two of the deposits identified by Ferring (1994) on the Trinity River in the Dallas-Fort Worth region of North Texas (of which Waxahachie Creek is a tributary): the Pilot Point alloformation and the Recent alluvium. The Pilot Point alloformation was deposited between approximately 4,500 BP and today, and Ferring notes that a prominent soil, the West Fork soil, has formed within the top of the Pilot Point. In some places, the West Fork is the surface soil and in other places buried by the Recent alluvium. The age of the West Fork soil is almost as wide, and the overall age range for the deposition of the Pilot Point alloformation, spanning between 3,900 and ~300 cal BP (see discussion in Shipp et al. 2019:77–82). In most published works, the West Fork soil has been identified in flood basin facies deposits, and in that depositional context, it lacks visible evidence of stratification and exhibits elevated organic carbon content, a slight decrease in calcium carbonate at its surface and a gradual increase with depth, and a subtle magnetic susceptibility enhancement (see Shipp et al. 2019; Sitters et al. 2016:7–10; Nickels 2014; Dayton et al. 2008; Frederick et al. 2006). 113


---

## Page 130

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Pemberton and Cliff (2008) speculated that all of the trenches excavated during survey exposed the West Fork soil, which Ferring (1994) describes having formed in the top of the Pilot Point alloformation. The stratigraphy exposed on the eastern wall reveals a more complex story that does not easily fit into Ferring’s stratigraphic narrative. Historic age sediments were deposited in the period of time ascribed to the Recent alluvium, and the majority of the deposits were emplaced in the period of time typically ascribed to the Pilot Point alloformation. Clear separation of these two different age deposit on the basis of a soil formed in the sediments is not apparent from the detailed dissection of the deposits performed here. A Comment on Excavation Methods The Gradall focused approach employed in excavating this site is best suited to flat-lying deposits where elevation can be used as a reasonable proxy for age. Where the deposits exhibit profound topographic variation as they do here (e.g., Zone 16 which traverses the entire eastern wall profile is inclined to the south at a rate of 8.5 cm per meter or ~4.9˚) greatly complicates resolving discrete analytical units and reaggregating the piece plotted assemblage for analysis. That the deposits appear to also dip to the west just compounds this problem. If the deposits exhibited prominent color or texture variations that could be easily followed with the mechanical excavation, then perhaps the method would have been more productive. But the extremely subtle variations in appearance revealed here were hard to see in optimum circumstances, and impossible to follow with the Gradall. Furthermore, we originally planned on one long stratigraphic key profile, but clearly two or more would have been better at revealing the complex three-dimensional nature of the deposits. With the benefit of hindsight, there are several things that could have improved the outcome here. First, having some latitude to explore the stratigraphy of the deposits before being locked into a block excavation would have been desirable. It now appears it is possible that the surface drainage traversing the site may be an augmented version of an infilled paleochannel and knowing that before doing the block excavation could have led to a very different scope of work. Furthermore, a testing phase that at least obtained some hand excavated units would have better prepared us for what we encountered. Going straight from trenching in survey to stripping omitted many details that would have been useful in planning and implementing the stripping. It may also be useful for TxDOT to consider exploring the best method to be used in teasing apart three-dimensional data sets such as this and then offering training to their consultants. Where sites have been excavated by hand, the assemblage exists in readily reassignable groups (levels within excavation units), and these can be relatively easily assigned analytical proveniences after the fact. But using just X, Y, Z coordinates to tease apart an assemblage is a task that was attempted with the Higgins Experiment (Black 1997) and is still not an easily accomplished task. If this site had more features, as was the case at the Fish Creek Site (Nickels 2014), then this would have been more easily accomplished. But this approach would seem to be less suited to light scatters which appear to dominate the archeological record here. 114


---

## Page 131

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Chapter 12: Summary and Recommendations The results of radiocarbon dating and the NRHP and SAL eligibility recommendations for Waxahachie Creek-1 site (41EL246) are presented in the following chapter. These discussions are followed by a preliminary set of recommendations for future data recovery investigations. Radiocarbon Dating Ten radiocarbon age estimates were obtained from 41EL246 (Figure 67 and Table 11). Five from the microartifact column, one each from bones at one of the highest elevation points and one of the lowest elevation points, as well as one from a burnt bone, possibly the result of human activity, and one from the cluster of bones on the southern end with the lithic flakes, in an attempt to date the lithics. In an attempt to provide context to the upper and lower levels of the site, two of the bone samples sent for dating were chosen to represent the high and low elevations of artifacts. These dates do not correspond with what was anticipated, but it is not clear why. Upper and lower date brackets are better represented by the microartifact column; however, as noted, this may not correspond in elevation across the site. Figure 67. Radiocarbon dates. 115

## Page 133

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 Corrected Lab δ13C Calibrated 2-sigma Sample Context Material Conventional Number ‰ Age Range 14C Age 1421–1498 A.D. Microartifact (90.5%) Column Beta - 1600–1616 A.D. Micro B Charcoal -26.1 430 ± 30 BP (120–125 577004 (4.3%) cmbs) 1506–1511 A.D. (0.6%) Research Questions Following the field investigations at 41EL246, an analytical research design was prepared based on historic contexts from Kenmotsu and Perttula (1993). The two relevant contexts are Quaternary Environments and Archeology in Northeast Texas and Hunter-Gatherer Mobility. Concepts that Kenmotsu and Perttula outline led to five research domains for this study: Site Chronology and Integrity; Settlement Patterns; Paleoenvironment; Subsistence Systems; and Material Culture and Technological Change. Research Domain 1: Site Chronology and Integrity 1. Research Question: What is the chronology of the site? Occupations at the site fall into three date ranges. The oldest dates to the Late Archaic, represented by Feature 1. No other dated materials from the site were of the same age. As noted in the geoarcheological analysis (see Chapter 11), this is either due to a discontinuity between Feature 1 and the other dated portions of the site, a different depositional regime, or a date on old wood. Least likely would be due to a date on old wood as the general maximum age of pecan and pine is approximately 300 years. Throughout the Late Prehistoric, there were a series of ephemeral occupations. This is seen in the microartifact column, with four probable occupations identified by lithic debitage, bone, burnt bone and shell, and charcoal. Lithic artifacts and burnt and butchered bone scattered throughout the site are likely also from this period given that the two bones potentially associated with human activity (one burnt and the other near lithic flakes) also date to the Late Prehistoric. Finally, the historic component dates to the mid- to late twentieth century and appears to be associated with the construction of commercial and industrial buildings in the immediate vicinity. 2. Research Question: What is the depositional history of the site? Geomorphological analysis of the eastern wall profile indicates that the deposits are typical of a channel fill or near channel depositional environment. One potential interpretation is a levee, with an elevated ground adjacent to the stream channel, and beds that dip away from the channel. Sediment would have built upwards and out to the south; however, the historic period sedimentation eliminated most of the difference in relief adjacent to the channel and on the floodplain to the south. It may have also been the fill of a paleochannel or cutoff channel, but without an east-west profile, there is insufficient evidence to assess this hypothesis. 117

## Page 134

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 3. Research Question: What is the stratigraphic integrity of the cultural deposits at the site? Geomorphological analysis of the site indicates that the bulk of the site has maintained stratigraphic integrity. Radiocarbon dates from the eastern wall profile are consistent with an intact profile, and there are no apparent disturbances in the profile. The magnetic susceptibility analysis of Feature 1 shows that that is intact as well. There is the possibility that the depression in the modern topography is a drainage cutting into a paleochannel, which could disturb cultural deposits, but that hypothesis is neither able to be confirmed nor rejected. The historic pit is a potential disturbance, especially as it had been burnt, though there was sedimentation between the prehistoric cultural deposits and the historic trash pit. The main question of integrity of the site is radiocarbon dates from the bones representing the high and low points of the deposit (FS 128 and 151) overlapping in age. It is not clear why these dates do not match what was anticipated, but they may indicate a disturbance in the site not captured by the eastern wall. Research Domain 2: Settlement Patterns 1. Research Question: How does the site tie into settlement patterns of the region? The sparse material remains on the site are consistent with a mobile settlement strategy. The microartifact analysis indicates the area was revisited multiple times, but it was not in constant use. Feature 1 represents a single-use heating feature, as one would expect from mobile hunter-gatherers during the Late Archaic. In the region, the Late Prehistoric shows a move from this mobile strategy to increasing population density and sedentism, with associated intensive land-use. The two Late Prehistoric dates from the block are an early date on the cusp of the Late Prehistoric, from the Richland Creek phase, and a somewhat later date, from the Round Prairie phase. Ceramics start to appear during the Richland Creek phase, with earth ovens and some structures, indicated by post holes. Though the overall subsistence pattern stays similar during the Round Prairie phase, increased sedentism and ceramic use is seen in the archeological record. These changes are not apparent in the cultural material recovered from the site, indicating the area may have been used as a short- term hunting camp rather than a habitation site. Specify multiple occupations throughout Late Prehistoric only have a few artifacts / ecofacts per occupation in the microartifact column. While this is only a small column on one part of the site, we would expect to have encountered additional features elsewhere in the block if this was a larger, more “sedentary” camp site. Research Domain 3: Paleoenvironment 1. Research Question: What is the paleoenvironment of the site? Snails recovered from flotation include Zonitoides arboreus (22 percent), Gastrocopta tappaniana (19 percent), and Gastrocopta pellucida (17 percent). These snails are common in leaf litter in wetlands and water-adjacent settings. The wood identified from the charcoal was possibly mulberry or mesquite. Notably, no oak was recovered, which is unusual for this time period. The faunal assemblage from the rest of the block conforms to what is expected 118


---

## Page 135

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 for Late Archaic to Late Prehistoric sites, with bison, deer, canids, reptiles, and other small mammals. The stable carbon isotopic record from the microartifact column suggests that prior to about 450 cal BP the valley floor was most likely either a grassland, or a savannah- like environment. Between about 300 and 500 years ago, there was a significant increase in organic matter contributed by C plants, most likely trees, followed by other phases of more 3 C -rich organic matter including around 200 years ago and recently. This implies that the 3 riparian ecosystem that Jurney et al. (1990:2–8) argue was present at the time of European settlement, specifically riparian forests comprised of elm, hackberry, Spanish oak, pecan, red haw, walnut, mesquite and mulberry, is a recent attribute of this environment. Research Domain 4: Subsistence Systems 1. Research Question: How does the site tie into regional subsistence patterns? Most of the information regarding subsistence patterns for site 41EL246 comes from the bone assemblage. The botanical remains only included wood samples from the flotation of Feature 1, most of which were too small to identify beyond hardwood. There was a piece that was either mesquite or mulberry; this sample dated to the Late Archaic, indicating this wood was most likely mulberry. While there were an abundance of shell, the majority of them were very small, likely associated with the environment and not a food source. None of the analyzed shell appeared to have been processed for food. There were a small number of bones that exhibited cultural modification, including burning, butchering, and boiling. Modification was observed on large mammals such as bison and deer; while small mammals and reptiles were present in the assemblage, none of them were modified. Small animals may, however, have been underrepresented since only the unit fill was screened. Focusing on large mammals is a low-intensive subsistence strategy consistent with relatively mobile populations of low density, but it should be noted that the excavation methodology favored the recovery of large animal bones. Research Domain 5: Material Culture and Technological Change 1. Research Question: What are the characteristics of the chipped lithic assemblage recovered from the site? There were six lithic debitage flakes and one thinned biface tool recovered from 41EL246. Two of the flakes were recovered from the flotation samples from Feature 1, three were recovered from the cluster of bone at the southern end of the site, and one flake was found isolated from the rest in the center of the site. Notably, the biface was not found in association with any of the flakes. One side of the biface is more refined than the other, with a relatively straighter side and finer flakes. It is not clear if it was an unfinished tool or only one side was intended to be utilized, but no signs of use were observed along that edge. The small size of the chipped lithic assemblage makes it impossible to make many observations regarding these activities on the site. It does confirm that people were engaging in lithic tool production or maintenance on the site. 119


---

## Page 136

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 In addition to the chipped stone, a single bone tool was recovered from the site. This was a fragment from a long bone shaft with one margin ground to sharp edge. While it resembles a scraper, its exact use is unknown. 2. Research Question: What are the characteristics of the fire-cracked rock recovered from the site? Feature 1 is the only feature that was confirmed to be the result of human activity; however, there is a large cluster of FCR on the eastern side that is likely the result of two root burns in close proximity. All FCR from Feature 1 was limestone-based, while the majority of the variability seen in the collection of FCR across the site was represented in the eastern cluster. Feature 1 contained stones that are very small by earth oven standards – only 12 stones were above the threshold to be reused again, indicating that most of these FCR were at the end of their lifecycle. Those 12 stones represent approximately a third of the weight of the total 1,216 pieces of FCR recovered from the site. Of the stones over a 0.5 in in size, n=88 (or 28 percent) appear to have been water-worn stones before they were heated. In other words, they did not exhibit multiple planes of fracture, if any at all, indicating that they were likely only heated once. Therefore, the FCR from Feature 1 is consistent with a single-use heating feature, though what specific type of heating feature is unclear. The cluster of FCR on the eastern end of the block, on the other hand, has relatively larger and blockier stones, though they are still small by oven standards. Thirteen of the 66 stones were large enough to reuse, but most of them were at the end of their potential lifecycle. If it were an earth oven, these stones would have been clast supported, not scattered vertically and horizontally as they are. The pattern of carbon stain in the profile is consistent with a root burn. If the FCR are present on the site due to human activity, it has been obscured by the root burn. Recommendations Site 41EL246 was a sparsley occupied site through the Late Archaic into the Late Prehistoric. It represents a series of temporary camps with no signficant accumulation of material culture. From the research perspective, a site like this is not particularily informative on its own but in aggregate with other sites where it can help inform what people were doing across a region. It does indicate that there are likely deeply buried, if transient, sites along the banks of Waxahachie Creek. The investigations reported here within the TxDOT APE have exhausted the site’s research potential and have mitigated the adverse effects of the proposed undertaking within the APE. It is difficult to offer interpretations regarding the nature of the portion of site 41EP246 investigated during the project, whether those interpretations involve the economic or settlement function of the site, its occupational history, or other aspects of the multiple layers of deposits with scattered distributions of prehistoric and historic materials. With the exception of one feature, most of the artifacts and ecofacts are broadly distributed vertically and horizontally throughout the excavation exposure. Clustered distributions indicating occupation surfaces or activity areas appear to be rare and, as established in Chapter 11, it is difficult to assign any such locations to specific geomorphic strata. The broad, low-density distributions of faunal bone, charcoal fragments, and a few chipped stone artifacts throughout the strata are likely a result of a combination of multiple deposition events resulting from very 120


---

## Page 137

Final Report – Archeological Data Recovery Investigations at Waxahachie Creek-1 Site (41EL246), Ellis County Work Authorization 57707SA004; Texas Antiquities Permit 8694 short-term occupations that were intermixed with the peripheral debris and artifact “toss zones” that are typically found around hunter-gatherer campsites. Numerous ethnographic and ethnoarcheological studies have observed patterns of artifact discard areas surrounding primary living spaces and activity areas (Binford 1978; Enloe et al. 1994; Hayden and Cannon 1983; Koetje 1994; Murray 1980; O’Connell 1987; O’Connell et al. 1991, 1992; Stevenson 1991; Yellen 1996). The dimensions and densities of refuse scatters vary according to several factors, including shifting residence, season of occupation, wind direction, reoccupation, and anticipated and actual mobility (after Kent 1991), but a recurrent pattern is size sorting among discard assemblages. Larger items and more hazardous objects tend to be tossed, swept, or otherwise cleaned and discarded some distance from activity areas or living spaces, while smaller items such as debitage tend to be overlooked and remain as primary refuse. The fact that most of the artifacts recovered during excavation are large, along with the nature of the faunal assemblage that consists of isolated fragments, tools, and various species, is particularly suggestive of toss zones and refuse disposal. Perhaps a more intensively occupied settlement is located within a small distance along Waxahachie Creek. 121

Black (2.5YR 2.5/, m; 10YR 4/2, d) silty clay, hard to firm, moderate medium subangular blocky structure, abrupt smooth boundary, 3 C 10YR 4/2 strongly effervescent, 10-15% coarse fragments consisting of rounded pea gravel, slag and limestone, common very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay coats on ped faces. ## Page 226

Versar, Inc. Page 2 of 7 Versar 2SA004.0011, Waxahachie 2019 Historic Artifact Data Depth FS Analytical Art. No. Unit Type (cm) No. Unit Class Type Dates Group Qty Analysis Comment Block - 282 3 14.01 Glass Clear 1929-1960 Domestic 1 Complete bottle with chipped and missing fragments from the rim of spout/opening. Horizontal seams on spout indicate twist cap. Bottle is four sided. Two sides are 73.45 MM in thickess and the two larger sides (the flat faces of the bottle) are 90.45 MM in thickess. Glass thickness is 63.60 MM in diameter. On two narrow edges of bottle, "The Chattanooga Medicine Co." is imprinted. On the flat faces of the bottle it is flanked with a decoative, raised seam on all four bottle edges. On the bottom of the bottle there is a oval. Inside the oval is a symbol of an "I" that is inside of a circle that is intertwined with a diamond. Next to this symbol is the number "2". The "I" symbol can be traced to the Owens-Illinos Glass Co from 1954- Present. Below the symbol and number "2" is a number "6". This bottle was likely to contain either of the two products that The Chattanooga Medicine Co. produced--"Black-drought" a senna based laxative developed in 1840 or "Wine of Cardui" a tonic targeted to women to help with discomfort of menstral cramps, like modern day Midol. https://sha.org/bottle/pdffiles/ILogoTable.pdf https://oneobjectblog.wordpress.com/2016/04/19/a- chattanooga-medicine-company-bottle/ https://www.fohbc.org/wp- content/uploads/2012/12/ChattanoogaMedCoJanFeb20 11.pdf Block - 258 3 14.01 Glass Clear ca. 1932-1952 Domestic 1 Complete glass bottle, no lid but horizontal seams indicate that the cap would be screwed on. Four-sided rectangular bottle that has three horizontal tiers at the bottom--they tiers are bulbous in form and protude further out that the top half of the bottle. On the bottom of the bottle, there is a letter "J" inside of a keystone followed by the numbers and letters "275-D". The "J" symbol came be traced to the Knox Glass Bottle Co. (Jackson) https://sha.org/bottle/pdffiles/JLogoTable.pdf https://sha.org/bottle/pdffiles/KnoxGlass.pdf

## Page 227

Versar, Inc. Page 3 of 7 Versar 2SA004.0011, Waxahachie 2019 Historic Artifact Data Depth FS Analytical Art. No. Unit Type (cm) No. Unit Class Type Dates Group Qty Analysis Comment Block - 327 3 14.01 Glass Clear ca. 1929-1960 Domestic 1 Complete, Round bottle with decorative vertical oval indentations on the bottom base area. Top spout is tappered with a chip missing from rim. No lid but horizontal seams around rim indicate a twist off cap. On the bottom of the bottle, there a letter "I" inside a circle that is interwined with a diamond--Indcating this bottle was manufactured by Owens-Illinois Glass Co. To the left of the symbol there is a "7" and to the right of the symbol is a "50" Below these markings, "Fitch" is imprinted. Below "Fitch" is a number "3". After combing through online several Ownes-Illinois Glass catalogues, we can infer that this bottle was likely used for comestics, oils, or hairproducts, which can be supported by the spout which has a slow, pouring mechanism. https://sha.org/bottle/pdffiles/ILogoTable.pdf https://sha.org/bottle/oi1933.htm Block - 194 3 14.01 Glass Clear C. 1940-1960 Domestic 4 In Four Pieces that fit together. Markings: Woosies Beverages REG. U.S. PAT. OFF. Real Juice Co. CAP 10 FL OZ 242-2 A LC 64 Dallas, Texas. Glass Thickness: 64 MM, Glass Diameter: 92 MM. Text is displayed in a banner design. Evidence of two horizontal seams, two vertical seams. Real Juice Co. was established in 1909. One of their popular flavors was a root beer soda called Woosie, named after their grandson, Robert Samuel Glazer, whose nickname was Woosie. During the 1940’s they expanded their product line and flavors included root beer, grape, strawberry, orange, lemonade, and cream soda. A six pack of Woosies sold for 19 cents and could be purchased at area 7-11’s, A & P, and Tom Thumb stores. During the 1950’s, the Woosie was arguably Dallas’ favorite soft drink. Nichols, 2012. https://www.dmagazine.com/food- drink/2012/08/history-of-dallas-food-the-woosie/

## Page 228

Versar, Inc. Page 4 of 7 Versar 2SA004.0011, Waxahachie 2019 Historic Artifact Data Depth FS Analytical Art. No. Unit Type (cm) No. Unit Class Type Dates Group Qty Analysis Comment Block - 242 3 14.01 Glass Clear ca. 1933-1980 Domestic 1 Markings: "Norwich" on three sides of triangular bottle, on bottom "1337, 0, Letter B inside of circle, 11". Norwich or Norwhich Pharmaceuticals Co. is a New York corporation, having its principal place of business at Norwich, New York. Glass Thickness: 63.35 MM, Glass Diameter: 85.27MM. Norwhich has been engaged in the business of manufacturing drug and pharmacal products. This bottle has a matching metal cap.The B in the circle on the bottom of the glass likely refers to "Brockway Glass Company", which dates this particular bottle to the 1930's-1980's. It is very likely that this bottle contained Peto-Bismal. "Pepto-Bismol is marketed in a clear colorless glass bottle, triangular in shape. The name "Norwich" is molded into the glass on each of its three sides, near the top. This form of container has been used since about 1925. http://oldglassbottles.blogspot.com/2012/08/norwich- glass-bottle.html http://oldglassbottles.blogspot.com/2012/09/blog- post.html https://www.leagle.com/decision/1958594167fsupp42 71511 https://sha.org/bottle/pdffiles/BLogoTable.pdf Block - 327 3 14.02 Glass Clear Activities 1 Complete, Clear, round-bottom scientific flask, 14 inch long neck.
